class="hidden" property="articleSection">research-article</div><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D5560%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DLiming%2BLing%252C%2BYing%2BBai%252C%2BZhaohua%2BWang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D6%26contentID%3Dacsami.7b17659%26title%3DRemarkable%2BEffect%2Bof%2BSodium%2BAlginate%2BAqueous%2BBinder%2Bon%2BAnatase%2BTiO2%2Bas%2BHigh-Performance%2BAnode%2Bin%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D5568%26publicationDate%3DFebruary%2B2018" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-176592_0011.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0011.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b17659&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sodium alginate (SA) is investigated as the aqueous binder to fabricate high-performance, low-cost, environmentally friendly, and durable TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) for the first time. Compared to the conventional polyvinylidene difluoride (PVDF) binder, electrodes using SA as the binder exhibit significant promotion of electrochemical performances. The initial Coulombic efficiency is as high as 62% at 0.1 C. A remarkable capacity of 180 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is achieved with no decay after 500 cycles at 1 C. Even at 10 C (3.4 A g<sup>–1</sup>), it remains 82 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 3600 cycles with approximate 100% Coulombic efficiency. TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes with SA binder display less electrolyte decomposition, fewer side reactions, high electrochemistry reaction activity, effective suppression of polarization, and good electrode morphology, which is ascribed to the rich carboxylic groups, high Young’s modulus, and good electrochemical stability of SA binder.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D5560%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DLiming%2BLing%252C%2BYing%2BBai%252C%2BZhaohua%2BWang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D6%26contentID%3Dacsami.7b17659%26title%3DRemarkable%2BEffect%2Bof%2BSodium%2BAlginate%2BAqueous%2BBinder%2Bon%2BAnatase%2BTiO2%2Bas%2BHigh-Performance%2BAnode%2Bin%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D5568%26publicationDate%3DFebruary%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290640&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Fluoropolymers" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Fluoropolymers" class="article__tags__link">Fluoropolymers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290689&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Oxides" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Oxides" class="article__tags__link">Oxides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Sodium" class="article__tags__link">Sodium</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291675&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Stability" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Stability" class="article__tags__link">Stability</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Alginate&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20alginate">sodium alginate</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Binder&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_binder">binder</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anatase+Tio2&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anatase%20TiO2">anatase TiO<sub>2</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Durable&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_durable">durable</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20batteries">sodium ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">1 Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Nowadays, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are quite attractive as power sources applied to smart grids, transportation systems, and other electric energy storage devices. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1, 2)</a> Compared to their high energy and power densities, the major concerns of LIBs are deficiency and unevenly distribution of lithium source. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> Taking into account the growing demand and market, these disadvantages will greatly restrict the application for future large-scale energy storage. While sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have received growing interest owing to the abundant and available advantages of sodium as an alternative to LIBs for large-scale energy storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> However, the larger ion radius (1.02 Å) of sodium than that of lithium (0.76 Å) affects the phase stability, reaction kinetics, and interphase formation. Therefore, the search for a suitable SIBs electrode material with low cost and high performance remains an obstacle. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> Recently, various materials have attracted great interest as SIBs cathode, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 6 to 16">(6-16)</a> such as layer transition metal oxides and polyanion-type compounds. Meanwhile, a variety of potential anode materials for SIBs has also been reported, for instance, carbonaceous, metal oxide, metal sulfide, metal alloy, and organic composite materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 17 to 25">(17-25)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Titanium-based oxides attract particularly extensive attention as SIBs and LIBs anode because of the reasonable cost, stability, nontoxicity, and suitable operation voltage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 26 to 31">(26-31)</a> As for SIBs anode, titanium dioxides (TiO<sub>2</sub>) with several polymorphs have been investigated successively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> Among them, with respect to anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>, the calculated activation energy for Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into the host structure is 0.52 eV, which is comparable to that of Li<sup>+</sup> (0.60 eV). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32, 33)</a> Thus, most works were reported using anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>, focusing on the studies of sodium storage mechanism and the improvements of electrochemical performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34 ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 34 to 36">(34-36)</a> Recently, Zhang et al. reported the nitrogen doped/carbon tuning yolk-like TiO<sub>2</sub>, resulting in fast electron transfer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Longoni et al. demonstrated the role of different exposed crystal facets on the sodium storage properties, thus overcoming the intrinsic limits of anatase transport properties by exposing the most favorable nanocrystal facets. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> Wu et al. fabricated a porous binary-phase anatase–TiO<sub>2</sub>–rutile–TiO<sub>2</sub> composite with high grain-boundary density, offering more interfaces for a novel interfacial storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Overall, these studies are almost based on the synthesis and modification of active material with the single or multi-strategy, only improving the intrinsically low electrical conductivity of TiO<sub>2</sub> by the elements doping, surface coating, composites constructing, nanomaterials/hierarchical structure design, and facet/phase optimizing. However, these methods are complicated in synthetic routes, sensitive to experiment conditions, the high cost, low reproducibility and undesirable scalability. Moreover, the batteries with TiO<sub>2</sub> obtained by the methods above suffer from a low initial Coulombic efficiency of less than 50%, resulting in the large loss of irreversible capacity. In fact, the battery performances can be promoted using excellent binder with appropriate swelling property, good electrochemical stability, high affinity, and homogeneous distribution of particles inside the electrode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 40 to 43">(40-43)</a> The binder is the key inactive component, which can greatly affect the contact between the active materials and the current collector, as well as the interparticles contact. Unfortunately, the effect of the binders on the sodium storage performance of TiO<sub>2</sub> has never been reported to the best of my knowledge.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Here, for the first time, we adopted sodium alginate (SA) as the binder to study its effect on the sodium storage performance of TiO<sub>2</sub>. SA is chosen due to its rich carboxylic groups, high Young’s modulus, good electrochemical stability, low cost, and nontoxicity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> Compared to the conventional PVDF binder, electrodes using SA as the binder exhibit significant promotion of the initial Coulombic efficiency, rate capability and cycling stability. The initial Coulombic efficiency is as high as 62% at 0.1 C. A remarkable capacity of 180 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is achieved with no decay after 500 cycles at 1 C. Even at 10 C (3.4 A g<sup>–1</sup>), it remains 82 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 3600 cycles with approximate 100% Coulombic efficiency.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">2 Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2-1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3">2.1 Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p">The anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> microparticles were synthesized via a solvothermal method. Typically, tetrabutyl titanate (TBOT, 0.25 mL) was added dropwise into acetic acid (HAc, 50 mL) under stirring. Then the white suspension was transferred to a 100-mL sealed Teflon reactor and heated to 210 °C for 24 h. After it was collected by centrifugation and washed with deionized (DI) water and ethanol several times, the obtained powder was dried at 60 °C for 24 h and, finally, calcined at 400 °C for 30 min in air.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF, −(−CH<sub>2</sub>–CF<sub>2</sub>−)<sub><i>n</i></sub>−) and sodium alginate (SA, (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>7</sub>O<sub>6</sub>Na)<sub><i>n</i></sub>) were acquired from ARKEMA, FRA and ALADDIN, CHN, respectively. The two kinds of polymer binders were used in the form of solution. PVDF solution was obtained by directly dissolving PVDF powder (5 wt %) in <i>N</i>-methyl pyrrolidone (NMP). SA solution was attained by dissolving SA powder (3 wt %) in DI water. Super-P was used as the carbon additive. The electrolyte was 1 M solution of NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in ethylene carbonate (EC) and propylene carbonate (PC) (1:1 v/v).</div></div><div id="sec2-2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.2 Cells</h3><div class="NLM_p last">To prepare the working electrode, TiO<sub>2</sub> microparticles, Super-P, and the binder (PVDF or SA) were mixed in a 7:2:1 wt. ratio. Then the obtained uniform slurry was coated on Cu foil, dried under vacuum at 120 °C for 24 h, and pressed at the pressure of 2 MPa. Two-electrode coin cells (CR2025) employing sodium metal as counter electrodes and glass fiber as separators were assembled in an Ar-filled glovebox.</div></div><div id="sec2-3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.3 Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">Crystallographic information was analyzed in the 2θ range from 10° to 90° by Rigaku2400 powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) with Cu Kα radiation source. The morphologies were characterized by a S-4800 field-emission scanning electron microscope (FE-SEM) and a Tecnai G<sup>2</sup> F20 high-resolution transmission electron microscope (HR-TEM). Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy measurements were performed using a Nicolet iS50 (Thermo Fisher Scientific Inc., USA).</div><div class="NLM_p last">Galvanostatic measurements were carried out on a LAND-CT2001A instrument, with 1 C = 335 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) experiments were conducted with a CHI660e electrochemical workstation. CV curves were recorded at a constant scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> between 0.02 and 2.5 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. Alternating current (AC) impedance measurements were performed over the frequency ranging from 0.1 Hz to 100 kHz using a small perturbation of ±5 mV.</div></div><div id="sec2-4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.4 Calculation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">We carried out first-principles calculations in the framework of density functional theory (DFT) as implemented in the Cambridge Sequential Total Energy Package (CASTEP). The generalized gradient approximation (GGA) as formulated by Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE) has been used for exchange and correlation contributions. A plane-wave basis set with an energy cutoff of 500 eV was used in the calculations with sufficient Monkhorst–Pack sampled <i>k</i> points 3 × 3 × 1. The convergence criterion of the self-consistent field calculations was set to 5 × 10<sup>–6</sup> eV/atom for the total energy. All structures were fully relaxed until the final force on each atom becomes less than 0.01 eV/Å. To prevent spurious interaction between isolated monolayers, a vacuum spacing of at least 15 Å was introduced and pressure on the supercell was decreased to values less than 0.02 GPa.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3 Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The chemical structure of SA is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>, along with that of PVDF for comparison. These structures were studied by Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a). For SA binder, the main absorbance bands at ∼1598, 1410, 1300, and 1028 cm<sup>–1</sup> are related to O–C–O (carboxylate) asymmetric vibrations, O–C–O symmetric vibrations, C–C–H (and O–C–H) deformation of pyranose rings and C–O–C asymmetric vibrations. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> In contrast to SA, the FTIR spectrum of PVDF shows the characteristic peaks at 1180 and 870 cm<sup>–1</sup>, which are ascribed to the stretching frequencies of CF<sub>2</sub>. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b shows the XRD patterns of these binders. PVDF binder displays the obvious diffraction peak at 20°, while SA presents no strong peaks, which reveals the better crystallinity of PVDF resulting from its semi crystalline polymer with a polymorphism. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> XRD pattern also confirms the anatase phase of the as-prepared TiO<sub>2</sub> sample, showing no impurities (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). The TiO<sub>2</sub> sample is composed of microparticles without uniform shape and ordered structure (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a). High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) displays the lattice fringes with an interplanar spacing of <i>d</i> = 0.35 nm, which correspond to the (101) planes (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b).</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-176592_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0010.jpeg" id="gr10" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Schematic Diagram of the Interactions of the Binder (PVDF or SA) with the Cu Foil, TiO<sub>2</sub> Active Materials, and the Electrolyte</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b17659&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-176592_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) FTIR spectra of PVDF and SA. (b) XRD patterns of PVDF, SA, and TiO<sub>2</sub>. The black vertical line is the standard spectrum of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS card no. 04-0477). The FTIR characteristic peaks of PVDF and SA are marked with red and blue asterisks, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b17659&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-176592_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. SEM (a) and HR-TEM (b) images of TiO<sub>2</sub> sample. SEM images of TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes using different binders: (c) PVDF and (d) SA.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b17659&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The PVDF or SA solution was attained by dissolving PVDF powder (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a) in NMP or SA powder (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b) in DI water, respectively. Both solutions in uniform distributions are translucent (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figures 3">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c, d). The TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes were prepared by using these binders, carbon additive, and TiO<sub>2</sub> particles. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e, the prepared electrode slices show the same appearances. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) studies further reveal that the distribution of materials is homogeneous and there are no visible cracks (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figures 2">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c, d). Moreover, as the energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) mapping shows in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/suppl_file/am7b17659_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>, the existence of PVDF or SA is confirmed by their special element of F or Na, respectively. It also further demonstrates the homogeneous distribution of PVDF or SA.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-176592_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Digital images of PVDF (a) and SA (b) powders, PVDF (c) and SA (d) solutions, and electrode slices using PVDF or SA binder (e).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b17659&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a and b show the discharge/charge curves of the cell using PVDF and SA binder at 0.1 C, respectively. Although the initial discharge capacities of TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF and TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode are almost the same with ∼390 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF, and TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrodes display the initial charge capacities of 161 and 241 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Thus, the initial Coulombic efficiency of TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode is 62%, which is much higher than that of TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF electrode (41.5%) and the previous studies (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>). For subsequent cycles, TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF electrode delivers obvious capacity decay and voltage changes, which are significantly different from TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c, the capacity retention of TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF electrode after 100 cycles at 0.1 C is only 43.5%, which is much lower than that of TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode and shows poor cycling stability. To further reveal the voltage changes, the average discharge/charge voltage was calculated. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d displays the calculated differences in the average charge and discharge voltage at 0.1 C during cycling. Generally, the calculated voltage difference of TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF electrode increases and the voltage difference of TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode decreases. The 100th average voltage is 0.37 and 1.28 V for the discharge and charge process of cell using PVDF binder, respectively. Nevertheless, the average voltage becomes 0.78 and 0.91 V for the discharge and charge process with TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode, respectively. In contrast to PVDF binder, the SA binder plays a key effect in enhancing the initial Coulombic efficiency, cycling stability and suppressing the polarization of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-176592_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Galvanostatic curves at 0.1 C for TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF (a) and TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA (b) anode. (c) The cycling stability at 0.1 C. (d) The differences in the average charge and discharge voltage at 0.1 C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b17659&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Initial Coulombic Efficiency of Previous Studies on Anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> as SIB Anode</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table" border="0"><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">ref</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">initial efficiency (%)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">ref</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">initial efficiency (%)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">ref</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">initial efficiency (%)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">47</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">36.3</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">48</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">&lt;30</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">49</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">46</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">50</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">∼40</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">51</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">38</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">52</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">31.4</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">53</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">∼30</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">54</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">46.1</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">55</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">38.2</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" aria-label="reference 56">56</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">∼50</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">57</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">∼40</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">58</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">∼35</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">The d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plots often contain sharp features and are more informative. Therefore, the d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plots at 0.1 C are provided in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>. In the first discharge process, TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF electrode displays the two clear reduction peaks at 0.97 and 0.62 V, which disappear in the subsequent cycles (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figures 5">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a, b). It is mainly ascribed to the electrolyte decomposition thus the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) and some side reactions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53 ref59 ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53 ref59 ref60" aria-label="reference 53, 59 and 60">(53, 59, 60)</a> The strong peak at 0.05 V in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a was demonstrated to be the irreversible phase transition of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> during initial discharge. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> In contrast, TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode mainly shows the stronger peak at 0.23 V in first discharge cycle related to the irreversible phase transition (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c), which is consistent with the obvious voltage plateau around 0.2 V (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b) and will vanish in the subsequent cycles. The formation of SEI films is less according to the weak peak at 0.59 V (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d). The electrolyte decomposition and side reactions can be inhibited by using SA as binder. Thus, TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode displays higher initial charge capacity and Coulombic efficiency (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figures 4">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a, b). For subsequent d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plots of TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF electrode (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b), obvious voltage change at the cathodic/anodic peaks is observed, which is similar to the trends of average voltage change as revealed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d. Additionally, during the cycling, the absolute value of peak intensity gradually decreases when using PVDF as the binder; the absolute value of peak intensity almost remains unchanged for TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode. It is consistent with the cycling stability differences of cells using PVDF and SA as the binder.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-176592_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plots obtained from the galvanostatic curves at 0.1 C for TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF (a, b) or TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA (c,d) anode. Panels a and c are the overall curves. Panels b and d are the enlarged curves of panels a and c, respectively. The peaks of electrolyte decomposition and side reactions are marked with black asterisks. The peaks of irreversible phase transition are marked with red asterisks. The red arrows reveal the changes at the cathodic/anodic peaks.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b17659&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The differences in the electrochemical performance using PVDF or SA as the binder were evaluated in detail (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>). The rate capability of the samples was tested from 0.1 to 20 C (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a). Even at 20 C, TiO<sub>2</sub> anode with SA binder exhibits high capacity of 82 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. In contrast, the cell using traditional PVDF binder delivers significantly lower capacities of only 8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 20 C. In particularly, TiO<sub>2</sub> anode with SA binder exhibits a satisfactory capacity of 180 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with no decay after 500 cycles at 1 C (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b). However, TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF anode shows poor stability and capacity (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b). Moreover, at a high rate of 10 C, the cell using SA binder displays high capacity of 82 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> even after 3600 cycles (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c). The average Coulombic efficiency is up to 99.97%. Clearly, the TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode using SA as the binder shows the excellent sodium storage capacity and super durable stability at high rates.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-176592_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Rate performance at the different rates and (b) cycling stability at 1 C of TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF or TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA anode. (c) Cycling stability at 10 C of cell using SA binder. The cells from panels b and c were tested after they were activated at 0.1 C for three cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b17659&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To investigate the effect of the binders on the electrode morphology, the electrodes were disassembled after cycling. We can observe the significant morphologies difference of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF and TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrodes after cycling (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>). Compared to the pristine electrodes (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c), the whole surface of TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF electrode is covered by a clearly visible film (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a). It demonstrates the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) films, causing the extra electrolyte decomposition and large capacity loss. In addition, such a thick and densely closed membranous layer will greatly affect the Na<sup>+</sup> ions migration within the SEI film, thus degrading the electrochemical performance. Specially, there are some obvious cracks (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b) on its surface, indicating the weak adhesion between particles and finally giving rise to a poor stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61" aria-label="reference 61">(61)</a> Nevertheless, the morphologies of TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode can still be maintained well after cycling, indicating an excellent structural stability (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figures 7">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c-d). Obvously, the TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF anode suffers from severe changes during the cycling.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-176592_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. SEM images of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF (a, b) and TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA (c, d) electrodes after cycling.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b17659&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Besides, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a> presents the cross-section images of the electrodes for TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF and TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA. When PVDF was used as the binder, a small gap is found between the electrode layer and the current collector (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a). The gap largely expands after cycling, causing current collector to detach (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>b). However, the TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode layer on the pristine electrode slice displays good adherence to the current collector (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>c), and tightly connected even after 500 cycles at 1 C (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>d). We also calculated the binding energy of the binder with Cu foil. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>, the binding energy of SA binder with Cu foil is 13.07 eV, which is much higher than <i>E</i><sub>PVDF-Cu</sub> (only 5.95 eV). It demonstrates the strong binding effect of SA binder with Cu foil, thus showing a favorable TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode morphology.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-176592_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Cross sections morphologies of the pristine TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF (a) and TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA (c) electrodes. Cross sections morphologies of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF (b) and TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA (d) electrodes after 500 cycles at 1 C. The layer of electrode materials is marked with the red arrow.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b17659&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To get insight into the robustness of the rate capability for TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode, cyclic voltammetry (CV) was performed as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figure 9">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>a. The cells were cycled for 3 times at 0.1 C before the CV test. A pair of peaks at 0.85 V (anodic) and 0.55 V (cathodic) is detected for TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF electrode, while these respective strong peaks are at 0.85 V (anodic) and 0.65 V (cathodic) for TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode. It corresponds to the reversible sodium ions storage in the TiO<sub>2</sub> material with the Ti<sup>3+</sup>/Ti<sup>4+</sup> redox. The current of TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode is much higher than the TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF electrode. TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode also displays the sharper peak shape and the lower voltage difference (0.2 V) in the oxidation reduction peak. It demonstrates the high electrochemical reaction activity, good reversibility, and small polarization of TiO<sub>2</sub> anode using SA as the binder. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" aria-label="reference 62">(62)</a></div><figure id="fig9" data-index="10" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/medium/am-2017-176592_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0009.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. (a) CV measurements of TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF and TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrodes with the 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> scan rate and the 0.02–2.5 V (versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) voltage range. The overall (b) and the enlarged (c) Nyquist plot of TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode using PVDF or SA as the binder. (d) The <i>Z</i>′ vs ω<sup>–<i>1/2</i></sup> plots in the low frequency region. (e) Equivalent circuit that is used to fit the experimental data.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/large/am-2017-176592_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b17659&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy is applied to reveal the detailed interfacial kinetics and polarization types of the electrode with PVDF or SA binder. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figures 9">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>b, c show the Nyquist plots of TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF and TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrodes after the fifth cycles. An intercept at the <i>Z</i>′ axis at high frequency indicates the ohmic resistance of the tested battery (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>). Both Nyquist plots display two depressed semicircles in the high and medium frequency region, showing similar shapes but obvious differences in the impedance values. The first semicircle corresponds to SEI surface impedance (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>) and constant phase element (CPE). The second depressed semicircle is associated with the Na ions transport through the interfacial charge transfer reaction (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) combined with the double-layer capacitance across the surface (<i>C</i><sub>dl</sub>). In addition, an approximately 45° slope line is found in the low-frequency region for the two electrodes, standing for the Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>W</sub>) related to the solid-state Na ions diffusion in the bulk of the active materials.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figure 9">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>e illustrates the typical equivalent model, which fits and interprets well to the impedance spectra for the electrodes after discharge. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref63'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a> The CPE, <i>C</i><sub>dl</sub>, and <i>C</i><sub>int</sub> are constant phase element, double-layer capacitance, and the interaction capacitance, respectively. The fitted impedance parameters are listed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl2" aria-label="Table 2">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">2</a>. The TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode exhibits rather lower impedance than TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF including the <i>R</i><sub>s</sub>, <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>, and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>. Therefore, SA can greatly enhance the SEI surface conductivity and electrochemical reactivity at the surface/interface region. SA plays a positive role in the charge transfer process, thus lowering the polarization of the TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl2"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 2. Properties of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF and TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA Electrodes from EIS Spectra</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table" border="0"><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">electrode</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>R</i><sub>s</sub> (Ω)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> (Ω)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> (Ω)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> (cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>–1</sup>)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">5.4</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">77.8</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">457.4</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">4.44&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>–15</sup></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.4</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">34.3</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">24.8</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">5.5&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>–13</sup></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">The apparent sodium diffusion coefficient, <i>D</i> (cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>), can be calculated from the inclined lines in the Warburg region using the equation<span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="ueq1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/asset/images/am-2017-176592_m001.gif" alt=""></span>where <i>R</i> is the gas constant, <i>T</i> is the absolute temperature, <i>A</i> is the surface area of the electrode, <i>n</i> is the charge-transfer number, <i>F</i> is the Faraday constant (96485 C mol<sup>–1</sup>), <i>C</i> is the concentration of Na ions in the solid (0.01625 mol cm<sup>–3</sup>), and σ is the Warburg factor that can be obtained by plotting in the complex plane <i>Z</i>′(real) = σω<sup>–1/2</sup> against ω<sup>–1/2</sup>, where ω is angular frequency (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig9" aria-label="Figure 9">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>d). The <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> of TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode is calculated to be 5.5 × 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, which is beyond 100 times significantly higher than TiO<sub>2</sub>/PVDF (4.44 × 10<sup>–15</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>). Here, since the diffusion depends on the concentration gradient, the apparent diffusion coefficient can be influenced by the different interfacial behaviors. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref64'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref64" aria-label="reference 64">(64)</a> The obvious differences in the electrochemistry performance can be ascribed to the 2 orders of magnitude increase of TiO<sub>2</sub>/SA electrode in apparent diffusion coefficient, along with the less charge transfer resistance and the decreasing resistance from the SEI layer.</div><div class="NLM_p last">As revealed above, the sodium storage performances of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> have been greatly promoted by using SA as the binder. The potential reasons are analyzed as follows and depicted in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>: (a) In a dry or wet (impregnated with electrolyte solvent) state, SA binder demonstrates to be a higher Young’s modulus, showing negligibly small swellability and weak polymer/electrolyte interaction, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> which contributes to preventing undesirable access of electrolyte liquid to the binder/TiO<sub>2</sub> interface, leading to the super durable stability. In contrast, during cycling, more and more PVDF binder will dissolve in the electrolytes, which causes the gelation of PVDF and electrolyte, and then leads to continuously covering on the electrode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref65'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref65" aria-label="reference 65">(65)</a> A membranous layer is so thick that it will greatly affect the Na<sup>+</sup> ions migration within the SEI film, thus degrading the electrochemical performance. (b) SA binder possesses the carboxylic groups with high concentration, uniform distribution and strong polarity in the polymer chain. The polar group can react with the hydroxyl groups on the surfaces of TiO<sub>2</sub> microparticles, forming the hydrogen bonding and thus strong adhesion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref66'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref66" aria-label="reference 66">(66)</a> As the FTIR spectra of SA binder shows, the 1300 cm<sup>–1</sup> peak is related to pyranose-ring deformation vibrations. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> After electrode formation with TiO<sub>2</sub>, its relative intensity obviously decreases (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/suppl_file/am7b17659_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>), indicating a chemical interaction between SA and TiO<sub>2</sub> particles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref67'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref67" aria-label="reference 67">(67)</a> However, for PVDF binder, the very weak hydrogen bond force with F and TiO<sub>2</sub> surface, and the weak van der Waals force result in relatively weak adhesion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref68'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref68" aria-label="reference 68">(68)</a> Furthermore, the high crystallinity of PVDF originated from its part bundled main chains can decrease the amount of PVDF binding site with TiO<sub>2</sub>, thus further lowering the adhesion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref69'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref69" aria-label="reference 69">(69)</a> The differences in molecular structures can highly influence the binding abilities of different binders, thus resulting in different changes in the electrode morphologies. (c) The polar hydrogen bonds between carboxyl groups of SA and the hydroxylated surface of TiO<sub>2</sub> also potentially contribute to forming the thin and stable SEI films, promoting a high CE and long cycle life. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref70'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref70" aria-label="reference 70">(70)</a></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">4 Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, for the first time, we developed a facile and scalable strategy utilizing SA as the binder to fabricate high-performance, low-cost, ecofriendly, and durable TiO<sub>2</sub> anode for SIBs. The SA binder plays the key role of a functional agent in enhancing the initial Coulombic efficiency, rate capability and cycling stability. TiO<sub>2</sub> anode with SA binder exhibits a remarkable capacity of 180 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with no decay after 500 cycles at 1 C. Even at 10 C (3.4 A g<sup>–1</sup>), it remains a capacity of 82 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 3600 cycles with 99.97% average Coulombic efficiency. It is attributed to the larger apparent diffusion coefficient, the less charge transfer resistance and the decreasing resistance from the SEI layer. In contrast, the cell using PVDF as the binder displays poor performance. Less electrolyte decomposition and side reaction, high electrochemistry reaction activity, effective suppression of polarization, and good electrode morphology depend on the special structure and characteristic of SA binder with rich carboxylic groups, high Young’s modulus, and good electrochemical stability. Our work not only proves that sodium alginate is effective and significant binder in TiO<sub>2</sub> anode but also delivers a new idea for improving electrochemical performances of SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.7b17659" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.7b17659</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) mapping of pristine TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes and FTIR spectra of SA/TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode (PDF) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/suppl_file/am7b17659_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/suppl_file/am7b17659_si_001.pdf">am7b17659_si_001.pdf (395.48 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b17659" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrZwDymuubZm7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljnUDv8g7quFw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhsVGktL%252FJ&amp;md5=5035bfda7631ad9d075e8836e61146ef</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201701083"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Luo, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, X.                 10.1039/C6CS00776G</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D46%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D3529-3614%26journal%3DChem.%2BSoc.%2BRev.%26author%3DJ.-Y.%2BHwang%26author%3DS.-T.%2BMyung%26author%3DY.-K.%2BSun%26title%3DSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%253A%2BPresent%2Band%2BFuture%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC6CS00776G&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b17659&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC6CS00776G&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot-2IKXj-Q-7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljnUDv8g7quFw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXkvF2rsbk%253D&amp;md5=cfc7b2d153696dcbded22d954ff06174</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40811f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hong, S. Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Park, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Choi, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Choi, N.-S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, K. T.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Charge Carriers in Rechargeable Batteries: Na Ions vs. Li Ions</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Energy Environ. Sci.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">6</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">2067</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">2081</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c3ee40811f</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D6%26publication_year%3D2013%26pages%3D2067-2081%26journal%3DEnergy%2BEnviron.%2BSci.%26author%3DS.%2BY.%2BHong%26author%3DY.%2BKim%26author%3DY.%2BPark%26author%3DA.%2BChoi%26author%3DN.-S.%2BChoi%26author%3DK.%2BT.%2BLee%26title%3DCharge%2BCarriers%2Bin%2BRechargeable%2BBatteries%253A%2BNa%2BIons%2Bvs.%2BLi%2BIons%26doi%3D10.1039%252Fc3ee40811f&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b17659&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2Fc3ee40811f&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Charge carriers in rechargeable batteries: Na ions vs. Li ions</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hong, Sung You; Kim, Youngjin; Park, Yuwon; Choi, Aram; Choi, Nam-Soon; Lee, Kyu Tae</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2067-2081</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  We discuss the similarities and dissimilarities of sodium- and lithium-ion batteries in terms of neg. and pos. electrodes.  Compared to the comprehensive body of work on lithium-ion batteries, research on sodium-ion batteries is still at the germination stage.  Since both sodium and lithium are alkali metals, they share similar chem. properties including ionicity, electronegativity and electrochem. reactivity.  They accordingly have comparable synthetic protocols and electrochem. performances, which indicates that sodium-ion batteries can be successfully developed based on previously applied approaches or methods in the lithium counterpart.  The electrode materials in Li-ion batteries provide the best library for research on Na-ion batteries because many Na-ion insertion hosts have their roots in Li-ion insertion hosts.  However, the larger size and different bonding characteristics of sodium ions influence the thermodn. and/or kinetic properties of sodium-ion batteries, which leads to unexpected behavior in electrochem. performance and reaction mechanism, compared to lithium-ion batteries.  This perspective provides a comparative overview of the major developments in the area of pos. and neg. electrode materials in both Li-ion and Na-ion batteries in the past decade.  Highlighted are concepts in solid state chem. and electrochem. that have provided new opportunities for tailored design that can be extended to many different electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqHBS0wIkomorVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li0q409aT1HgA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtVWmtL%252FE&amp;md5=023136cc0e5ed94e18ce0d526a0000a4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1039/C4EE03192J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Han, M. H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gonzalo, E.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Singh, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Rojo, T.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Comprehensive Review of Sodium Layered Oxides: Powerful Cathodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Energy Environ.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A comprehensive review of sodium layered oxides: powerful cathodes for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Man Huon; Gonzalo, Elena; Singh, Gurpreet; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">81-102</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
