                        cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Highly Ordered Three-Dimensional Ni-TiO2 Nanoarrays as Sodium Ion Battery Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Yang; Zhou, Min; Wen, Liaoyong; Wang, Chengliang; Zhao, Huaping; Mi, Yan; Liang, Liying; Fu, Qun; Wu, Minghong; Lei, Yong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">27</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4274-4280</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (SIBs) represent an effective energy storage technol. with potentially lower material costs than Li ion batteries.  Here, the electrochem. performance of SIBs, esp. rate capability, is intimately connected to the electrode design at the nanoscale by taking anatase TiO2 as an example.  Highly ordered 3-dimensional (3D) Ni-TiO2 core-shell nanoarrays were fabricated using nanoimprited AAO templating technique and directly used as anode.  The nanoarrays delivered a reversible capacity of ∼200 mA h g-1 after 100 cycles at the c.d. of 50 mA h g-1 and were able to retain a capacity of ∼95 mA h g-1 at the c.d. ≤5 A g-1 and fully recover low rate capacity.  High ion accessibility, fast electron transport, and excellent electrode integrity were shown as great merits to obtain the presented electrochem. performance.  The work demonstrates the possibility of highly ordered 3-dimensional heterostructured nanoarrays as a promising electrode design for Na energy storage to alleviate the reliance on the materials' intrinsic nature and provides a versatile and cost-effective technique for the fabrication of such perfectly ordered nanostructures.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoHslR0Alvw9bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljWOMLt_YnKWg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXpsFOjuro%253D&amp;md5=2843c505872489891b1e7f89f12fa7a3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit54" data-doi="10.1038/srep11960"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hong, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wei, M.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Iso-Oriented Anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> Mesocages as a High Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Storage</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Sci.                         cas_cit54R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">54</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Iso-Oriented Anatase TiO2 Mesocages as a High Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hong Zhensheng; Zhou Kaiqiang; Huang Zhigao; Wei Mingdeng; Wei Mingdeng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Scientific reports</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11960</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">A major obstacle in realizing Na-ion batteries (NIBs) is the absence of suitable anode materials.  Herein, we firstly report the anatase TiO2 mesocages constructed by crystallographically oriented nanoparticle subunits as a high performance anode for NIBs.  The mesocages with tunable microstructures, high surface area (204 m(2) g(-1)) and uniform mesoporous structure were firstly prepared by a general synthesis method under the assist of sodium dodecyl sulfate (SDS).  It's notable that the TiO2 mesocages exhibit a large reversible capacity and good rate capability.  A stable capacity of 93 mAhg(-1) can be retained after 500 cycles at 10 C in the range of 0.01-2.5 V, indicating high rate performance and good cycling stability.  This could be due to the uniform architecture of iso-oriented mesocage structure with few grain boundaries and nanoporous nature, allowing fast electron and ion transport, and providing more active sites as well as freedom for volume change during Na-ion insertion.  CV measurements demonstrate that the sodium-ion storage process of anatase mesocages is mainly controlled by pseudocapacitive behavior, which is different from the lithium-ion storage and further facilitates the high rate capability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcQNy5_7QVOqG5Pq5LRczm8ffW6udTcc2ea0XnQDj_ZtPrntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC28%252FgvFWrtQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=0121d7bef5cb80893b9c3204ed716cc4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref55"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">55</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit55" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201501489"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tahir, M.                         cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Extraordinary Performance of Carbon-Coated Anatase TiO2 as Sodium-Ion Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tahir Muhammad Nawaz; Panthofer Martin; Tremel Wolfgang; Oschmann Bernd; Buchholz Daniel; Dou Xinwei; Passerini Stefano; Lieberwirth Ingo; Zentel Rudolf</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced energy materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1501489</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6832</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">The synthesis of in situ polymer-functionalized anatase TiO2 particles using an anchoring block copolymer with hydroxamate as coordinating species is reported, which yields nanoparticles (≈11 nm) in multigram scale.  Thermal annealing converts the polymer brushes into a uniform and homogeneous carbon coating as proven by high resolution transmission electron microscopy and Raman spectroscopy.  The strong impact of particle size as well as carbon coating on the electrochemical performance of anatase TiO2 is demonstrated.  Downsizing the particles leads to higher reversible uptake/release of sodium cations per formula unit TiO2 (e.g., 0.72 eq.  Na(+) (11 nm) vs only 0.56 eq.  Na(+) (40 nm)) while the carbon coating improves rate performance.  The combination of small particle size and homogeneous carbon coating allows for the excellent electrochemical performance of anatase TiO2 at high (134 mAh g(-1) at 10 C (3.35 A g(-1))) and low (≈227 mAh g(-1) at 0.1 C) current rates, high cycling stability (full capacity retention between 2nd and 300th cycle at 1 C) and improved coulombic efficiency (≈99.8%).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcRca10rw1_Tc0Gy1nwu7K2mfW6udTcc2eYxVouO0ojaY7ntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC1c%252Fht1Wltw%253D%253D&amp;md5=29c3c300fc394f1c426270e5e28f600e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit56" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201504412"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ni, J.                 10.1002/adma.201504412</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D28%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D2259-2265%26journal%3DAdv.%2BMater.%26author%3DJ.%2BF.%2BNi%26author%3DS.%2BD.%2BFu%26author%3DC.%2BWu%26author%3DJ.%2BMaier%26author%3DY.%2BYu%26author%3DL.%2BLi%26title%3DSelf-Supported%2BNanotube%2BArrays%2Bof%2BSulfur-Doped%2BTiO2%2BEnabling%2BUltrastable%2Band%2BRobust%2BSodium%2BStorage%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadma.201504412&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b17659&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadma.201504412&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit56R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">56</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Self-Supported Nanotube Arrays of Sulfur-Doped TiO2 Enabling Ultrastable and Robust Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ni, Jiangfeng; Fu, Shidong; Wu, Chao; Maier, Joachim; Yu, Yan; Li, Liang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">28</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2259-2265</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADVMEW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0935-9648</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We, for the 1st time, combine the unique nanotubular architecture with high cond. by engineering self-supported nanotube arrays of S-doped TiO2 (S-TiO2).  Such doping by isovalent substitution can be helpful owing to either elastic effect or, at high enough concn., to variation of materials const. (e.g., bandgap).  S-doped TiO2 materials have been reported and characterized, yet never used as electrodes for Na-based battery behaviors.  The increase of electronic cond. could be predominately ascribed to structure affects rather than proper doping, as shown by the strong effect on the band gap.  An addnl. possibility is the introduction of an anionic deficiency (anion vacancies compensated by electron due to prepn.).  Note the deoxygenation effect of S treatment, an aliovalent effect as, e.g., achieved by donor doping TiO2, may be excluded here.  The S-TiO2 nanotube array features intrinsic merits for Na storage.  First, the self-supported nanotube array can be directly adapted for electrode without using binder and C additive.  Second, the ordered nanotube architecture exhibits large interface and channels for transfer of Na ion during cycling.  Third, the S doping drastically boosts the electron movement within the nanotubes.  Consequently, the S-TiO2 nanotubes enable superior Na storage in terms of high capacity (320 mAh/g), ultrastable cycling (91% retention over 4400 cycles), and robust rate capability (167 mAh/g at 3.35 A/g).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrObwc1x3fRibVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljkt5JuhZLEaw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xht1ektbc%253D&amp;md5=cf8583f1f9e0593bbc7f6af9a5227e30</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref57"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">57</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit57" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201600606"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, X. W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, Z. Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yu, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nitrogen-Doped Ordered Mesoporous Anatase TiO2 Nanofibers as Anode Materials for High Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Small</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">12</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">3522</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">3529</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit57R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">57</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nitrogen-Doped Ordered Mesoporous Anatase TiO2 Nanofibers as Anode Materials for High Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Ying; Liu, Xiaowu; Yang, Zhenzhong; Gu, Lin; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">26</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3522-3529</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SMALBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1613-6810</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In summary, nitrogen-doped ordered mesoporous TiO2 nanofibers (N-MTO) have been fabricated by electrospinning method and subsequent nitridation treatment.  When used as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries, the N-MTO anode exhibits superior cycling stability and rate performance.  The 1D N-MTO contains nanosized TiO2 particles and pores that not only shorten the diffusion length of Na ions in TiO2 but also allow sufficient infiltration of electrolyte and allow fast diffusion of Na ions.  The introduction of N into TiO2 results in partial formation of Ti3+ assocd. with oxygen vacancies, leading to reduced sodium ion and electron transport diffusion resistance, which improves the rate capability at high rates.  The N-MTO shows an excellent rate capability (110 mAh/g at 10 C) and long cycle life (only 6% capacity loss after 500 cycles at a rate of 10 C).  The proposed N-MTO hybrid could serve as a model for simple modification of other metal oxide (i.e., CoO, Fe2O3) materials derived from electrospinning for potential application in energy storage systems and other field (such as photocatalysis).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpnXuplwQMaG7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljkt5JuhZLEaw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XotFOjt7k%253D&amp;md5=4fbefc1171bed1cd934f6a3165e25f72</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref58"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">58</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit58" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2017.05.040"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, W.                         cas_cit62R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">62</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">One-Pot Synthesized Bicontinuous Hierarchical Li3V2(PO4)3/C Mesoporous Nanowires for High-Rate and Ultralong-Life Lithium-ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wei, Qiulong; An, Qinyou; Chen, Dandan; Mai, Liqiang; Chen, Shiyu; Zhao, Yunlong; Hercule, Kalele Mulonda; Xu, Lin; Minhas-Khan, Aamir; Zhang, Qingjie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-ion batteries have attracted enormous attention for large-scale and sustainable energy storage applications.  Here the authors present a design of hierarchical Li3V2(PO4)3/C mesoporous nanowires via 1-pot synthesis process.  The mesoporous structure is directly in situ carbonized from the surfactants (CTAB and oxalic acid) along with the crystn. of Li3V2(PO4)3 without using any hard templates.  As a cathode for lithium-ion battery, the Li3V2(PO4)3/C mesoporous nanowires exhibit outstanding high-rate and ultralong-life performance with capacity retention of 80.0% after 3000 cycles at 5 C in 3-4.3 V.  Even at 10 C, it still delivers 88.0% of its theor. capacity.  The ability to provide this level of performance is attributed to the hierarchical mesoporous nanowires with bicontinuous electron/ion pathways, large electrode-electrolyte contact area, low charge transfer resistance, and robust structure stability upon prolonged cycling.  The unique mesoporous nanowires structure is favorable for improving the cyclability and rate capability in energy storage applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpe62JH_xzkX7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljQXoRlaL2qrA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXpt1KksQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=aafed2e144d4eae1cd187ee83893a458</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref63"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref63'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">63</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit63" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201302915"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Raji, A.-R.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A unique hierarchical structure composite of graphene-wrapped MnO2-graphene nanoribbons (GMG) was prepd.  In this composite, graphene flakes tightly sandwiched nanosized MnO2 that grew directly on the graphene nanoribbons.  Electrochem. expts. demonstrate that the GMG exhibits enhanced specific capacity and improved cycling stability as an anode material compared to MG and pure MnO2 because of the synergic effect between the graphene, GNRs, and MnO2.                         cas_cit64R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">64</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Diffusion behavior of sodium ions in Na0.44MnO2 in aqueous and non-aqueous electrolytes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Dong Jun; Ponraj, Rubha; Kannan, Aravindaraj G.; Lee, Hyun-Wook; Fathi, Reza; Ruffo, Riccardo; Mari, Claudio M.; Kim, Do Kyung</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The slow kinetics of bigger-sized sodium ions in intercalation compds. restricts the practical applications of sodium batteries.  In this work, sodium ion intercalation/deintercalation behavior of Na0.44MnO2 (NMO), which is one of the promising cathode materials for sodium batteries, is presented in both aq. and non-aq. electrolyte systems.  The NMO samples synthesized using modified Pechini method shows better rate capability in 0.5 M sodium sulfate aq. electrolyte system than the 1 M sodium perchlorate non-aq. system.  The difference in the rate performance is extensively investigated using electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements and the apparent diffusion coeffs. of sodium in NMO are detd. to be in the range of 1.08 × 10-13 to 9.15 × 10-12 cm2 s-1 in aq. system and in the range of 5.75 × 10-16 to 2.14 × 10-14 cm2 s-1 in non-aq. systems.                         cas_cit65R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">65</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Study of styrene butadiene rubber and sodium methyl cellulose as binder for negative electrodes in lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Buqa, H.; Holzapfel, M.; Krumeich, F.; Veit, C.; Novak, P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

                        cas_cit66R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">66</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Study on Polymer Binders for High-Capacity SiO Negative Electrode of Li-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Komaba, Shinichi; Shimomura, Keiji; Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Ozeki, Tomoaki; Yui, Hiroharu; Konno, Kohzo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-capacity SiO powder composite electrodes for rechargeable Li-ion batteries are prepd. with different polymer binders of poly(acrylic acid) (PAA), poly(vinyl alc.) (PVA), Na CM-cellulose (CMCNa), and conventional poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVdF).  Electrode performance of the SiO composites depends on the binder, and their electrochem. reversibility is improved by using PAA as the binder in comparison to the PVdF, CMCNa, and PVA binders.  Coulombic efficiency at the initial cycle is improved for the SiO-PAA composite electrode, and the reversible capacity reaches 700-750 mA-h/g for continuous fifty cycling test at a rate of 100 mA/g.  The improvement mechanism of SiO-PAA composite electrode is characterized by x-ray diffraction, electron microscopy, XPS, IR spectroscopy, self-discharge test, and adhesive strength test.  Amorphous PAA polymer not only tightly binds but also covers the individual SiO particles.  Also, the PAA binder suppresses swelling of the composite electrode with the electrolyte soln. compared to the PVdF binder.  Through-thickness elec. resistance of the PAA composite electrode is much lower than that of the PVdF when it is wet with the electrolyte.  Probably these characters of the PAA binder effectively suppress isolation of the SiO powders in the composite electrode assocd. with the large vol. expansion/shrinkage during the lithiation/de-lithiation processes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrfWvZ1NtMFrrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li2zY6YF405Ig"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXns1Knurs%253D&amp;md5=5a172e0505ab2e5895bff686cd9f67b5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref67"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref67'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">67</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit67" data-doi="10.1016/j.carres.2009.12.010"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Papageorgiou, S.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In this study, we investigated the potential of highly polar polyacrylonitrile (PAN) as a binder for neg. electrodes in lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).  The electrochem. performance of PAN on three representative active materials, com. graphite, high-capacity silicon/graphite, and high-power Li4Ti5O12 (LTO), was characterized and compared to the performance of conventional polyvinylidene fluoride (PVdF) and sodium-CM-cellulose (CMC) binders.  The high polarity of nitrile groups in PAN leads to larger binder coverage on the surface of active materials, resulting in strong adhesion and low irreversible capacity.  Addnl., PAN-contg. electrodes showed good electrolyte wettability and low charge transfer resistance.  These contribute to excellent electrochem. performance of PAN binder.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpyYZV5cHsBIbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li2zY6YF405Ig"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXktFajtLk%253D&amp;md5=068884b9d1eb6246a22bfd650dddf3e0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref69"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref69'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">69</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit69" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201100236"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shimomura, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shimbe, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ozeki, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Son, J.-Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Oji, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Katayama, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Miura, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Graphite-Silicon-Polyacrylate Negative Electrodes in Ionic Liquid Electrolyte for Safer Rechargeable Li-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv. Energy Mater.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2011</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">1</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">759</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">765</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.201100236</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D1%26publication_year%3D2011%26pages%3D759-765%26journal%3DAdv.%2BEnergy%2BMater.%26author%3DN.%2BYabuuchi%26author%3DK.%2BShimomura%26author%3DY.%2BShimbe%26author%3DT.%2BOzeki%26author%3DJ.-Y.%2BSon%26author%3DH.%2BOji%26author%3DY.%2BKatayama%26author%3DT.%2BMiura%26author%3DS.%2BKomaba%26title%3DGraphite-Silicon-Polyacrylate%2BNegative%2BElectrodes%2Bin%2BIonic%2BLiquid%2BElectrolyte%2Bfor%2BSafer%2BRechargeable%2BLi-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Faenm.201100236&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b17659&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Faenm.201100236&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit69R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">69</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Graphite-silicon-polyacrylate negative electrodes in ionic liquid electrolyte for safer rechargeable Li-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Shimomura, Keiji; Shimbe, Yukako; Ozeki, Tomoaki; Son, Jin-Young; Oji, Hiroshi; Katayama, Yasushi; Miura, Takashi; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">759-765</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium poly(acrylate) binder efficiently improves the electrode performance of graphite/silicon composites in TFSA-based pure ionic liq.  The reversible cycling with capacity of 360 mA-h/g is achieved using a graphite-PANa electrode and increases to 1000 mA-h/g with graphite/Si-PANa composite electrodes.  The electrode-active materials are covered with PANa binder, and thus agglomeration is suppressed.  The PANa layer prevents the decompn. of IL during electrochem. cycling, which probably behaves like a SEI.  High-energy cathode materials also reversibly works in the BMPTFSA system - reported elsewhere.  The results are important for high-energy and safe lithium-ion batteries with ionic liq.-based electrolytes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp4l9hPmgjyTLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li2zY6YF405Ig"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhtlShtrfK&amp;md5=2ae9693b2d056e8af240a70023be8c9a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref70"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref70'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">70</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit70" data-doi="10.1038/nnano.2012.35"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chan, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Choi, J. W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ryu, I.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mcdowell, M. T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, S. W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jackson, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, L.</span><span class="NLM_etal"></span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Stable Cycling of Double-Walled Silicon Nanotube Battery Anodes through Solid-Electrolyte Interphase Control</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nat. Nanotechnol.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">7</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">310</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">315</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/nnano.2012.35</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D7%26publication_year%3D2012%26pages%3D310-315%26journal%3DNat.%2BNanotechnol.%26author%3DH.%2BWu%26author%3DG.%2BChan%26author%3DJ.%2BW.%2BChoi%26author%3DI.%2BRyu%26author%3DY.%2BYao%26author%3DM.%2BT.%2BMcdowell%26author%3DS.%2BW.%2BLee%26author%3DA.%2BJackson%26author%3DY.%2BYang%26author%3DL.%2BHu%26title%3DStable%2BCycling%2Bof%2BDouble-Walled%2BSilicon%2BNanotube%2BBattery%2BAnodes%2Bthrough%2BSolid-Electrolyte%2BInterphase%2BControl%26doi%3D10.1038%252Fnnano.2012.35&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b17659&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fnnano.2012.35&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit70R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">70</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Stable cycling of double-walled silicon nanotube battery anodes through solid-electrolyte interphase control</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Hui; Chan, Gerentt; Choi, Jang Wook; Ryu, Ill; Yao, Yan; McDowell, Matthew T.; Lee, Seok Woo; Jackson, Ariel; Yang, Yuan; Hu, Liangbing; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">310-315</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NNAABX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1748-3387</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Although the performance of lithium ion-batteries continues to improve, their energy d. and cycle life remain insufficient for applications in consumer electronics, transport, and large-scale renewable energy storage.  Silicon has a large charge storage capacity and this makes it an attractive anode material, but pulverization during cycling and an unstable solid-electrolyte interphase has limited the cycle life of silicon anodes to hundreds of cycles.  Here, it is shown that anodes consisting of an active silicon nanotube surrounded by an ion-permeable silicon oxide shell can cycle over 6,000 times in half cells while retaining &gt; 85% of their initial capacity.  The outer surface of the silicon nanotube is prevented from expansion by the oxide shell, and the expanding inner surface is not exposed to the electrolyte, resulting in a stable solid-electrolyte interphase.  Batteries contg. these double-walled silicon nanotube anodes exhibit charge capacities approx. eight times larger than conventional carbon anodes and charging rates of up to 20C (a rate of 1C corresponds to complete charge or discharge in one hour).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqaEH2gqjH0ZbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljm1BM_TYfRLQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XksVentLc%253D&amp;md5=13d632e4d2279068afe4a35d7fe7d4c0</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrZwDymuubZm7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj7X9YkWew5lQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhsVGktL%252FJ&amp;md5=5035bfda7631ad9d075e8836e61146ef</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201701083"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Luo, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, X.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Ponders on the value of lithium, an element known for about 200 years, whose importance is now fast increasing in view of the promises it holds for energy storage and elec. cars.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo9wUp2-vV0PLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj7X9YkWew5lQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXmtlWltro%253D&amp;md5=7ec1d363de663b367de8885c86bafeb0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1039/C6CS00776G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hwang, J.-Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Myung, S.-T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, Y.-K.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium-Ion Batteries: Present and Future</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem. Soc. Rev.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">46</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">3529</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">3614</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C6CS00776G</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot-2IKXj-Q-7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj7X9YkWew5lQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXkvF2rsbk%253D&amp;md5=cfc7b2d153696dcbded22d954ff06174</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40811f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hong, S. Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Park, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Choi, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Choi, N.-S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, K.                         rightTab-cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Charge carriers in rechargeable batteries: Na ions vs. Li ions</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hong, Sung You; Kim, Youngjin; Park, Yuwon; Choi, Aram; Choi, Nam-Soon; Lee, Kyu Tae</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  We discuss the similarities and dissimilarities of sodium- and lithium-ion batteries in terms of neg. and pos. electrodes.  Compared to the comprehensive body of work on lithium-ion batteries, research on sodium-ion batteries is still at the germination stage.  Since both sodium and lithium are alkali metals, they share similar chem. properties including ionicity, electronegativity and electrochem. reactivity.  They accordingly have comparable synthetic protocols and electrochem. performances, which indicates that sodium-ion batteries can be successfully developed based on previously applied approaches or methods in the lithium counterpart.  The electrode materials in Li-ion batteries provide the best library for research on Na-ion batteries because many Na-ion insertion hosts have their roots in Li-ion insertion hosts.  However, the larger size and different bonding characteristics of sodium ions influence the thermodn. and/or kinetic properties of sodium-ion batteries, which leads to unexpected behavior in electrochem. performance and reaction mechanism, compared to lithium-ion batteries.  This perspective provides a comparative overview of the major developments in the area of pos. and neg. electrode materials in both Li-ion and Na-ion batteries in the past decade.  Highlighted are concepts in solid state chem. and electrochem. that have provided new opportunities for tailored design that can be extended to many different electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqHBS0wIkomorVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj7X9YkWew5lQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtVWmtL%252FE&amp;md5=023136cc0e5ed94e18ce0d526a0000a4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1039/C4EE03192J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Han, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The room temp. Na-ion secondary battery has been under focus lately due to its feasibility to compete against the already well-established Li-ion secondary battery.  Although there are many obstacles to overcome before the Na-ion battery becomes com. available, recent research discoveries corroborate that some of the cathode materials for the Na-ion battery have indeed indisputable advantages over its Li-ion counterparts.  In this publication, a comprehensive review of layered oxides (NaTMO2, TM = Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, and a mixt. of 2 or 3 elements) as a viable Na-ion battery cathode is presented.  Single TM systems are well characterized not only for their electrochem. performance but also for their structural transitions during the cycle.  Binary TM systems are investigated in order to address issues regarding low reversible capacity, capacity retention, operating voltage, and structural stability.  As a consequence, some materials already have reached an energy d. of 520 mW h g-1, which is comparable to that of LiFePO4.  Furthermore, some ternary TM systems retained more than 72% of their capacity along with over 99.7% Coulombic efficiency for 275 cycles.  The goal of this review is to present the development of Na layered oxide materials in the past as well as the state of the art today in order to emphasize the compatibility and durability of layered oxides as powerful candidates for Na-ion battery cathode materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpwo96Ash7WNbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj7X9YkWew5lQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXitVCrsLjE&amp;md5=d938be66a2a0948e3d2f589b803dbf76</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref7" class="rightTabRefNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit7" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201501527"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiang, X.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Na-ion batteries (SIBs) receive significant attention for electrochem. energy storage and conversion owing to their wide availability and the low cost of Na resources.  However, SIBs face challenges of low specific energy, short cycling life, and insufficient specific power, owing to the heavy mass and large radius of Na+ ions.  As an important component of SIBs, cathode materials have a significant effect on the SIB electrochem. performance.  The most recent advances and prospects of inorg. and org. cathode materials are summarized here.  Among current cathode materials, layered transition-metal oxides achieve high specific energies around 600 mW h g-1 owing to their high specific capacities of 180-220 mA h g-1 and their moderate operating potentials of 2.7-3.2 V (vs. Na+/Na).  Porous Na3V2(PO4)3/C nanomaterials exhibit excellent cycling performance with almost 100% retention over 1000 cycles owing to their robust structural framework.  Recent emerging cathode materials, such as amorphous NaFePO4 and pteridine derivs. show interesting electrochem. properties and attractive prospects for application in SIBs.  Future work should focus on strategies to enhance the overall performance of cathode materials in terms of specific energy, cycling life, and rate capability with cationic doping, anionic substitution, morphol. fabrication, and electrolyte matching.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRG1Bub04s8LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgsFMWiWdDSwA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtlCjur7N&amp;md5=6d0a96f9e9421c02a9f3b48744267752</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref8" class="rightTabRefNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit8" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201501727"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fang, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, Y.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In order to get an element substituted into Na3V2(PO4)3/C in an appointed V site, the simple sol-gel method is used to design and prep. a series of Na-rich Na3+xV2-xNix(PO4)3/C (x = 0-0.07) compds.  To get a charge balance, the ratio of Na, V, and Ni would be changed if Ni goes into a different site.  Hence, ICP is applied to probe the real stoichiometry of the as-prepd. Na3+xV2-xNix(PO4)3/C (x = 0-0.07).  According to the Na/V ratio from the ICP result, it indicates that Ni2+ goes to a V site, and more Na+ will be introduced into the crystal to keep the charge balance.  In addn., the crystal structure changes are explored by XRD and Rietveld refinement, it is indicated from the results that Ni2+ doping does not destroy the lattice structure of Na3V2(PO4)3.  When applied as Na-storage material, the electrochem. property of all Ni2+ doped Na3+xV2-xNix(PO4)3/C composites have been significantly improved, esp. for the Na3.03V1.97Ni0.03(PO4)3/C sample.  For example, 107.1 mAh g-1 can be obtained at the first cycle; after 100 cycles, the capacity retention is as high as 95.5%.  Moreover, when charging/discharging at a higher rate of 5 C, the capacity still remains 88.9 mAh g-1, displaying good rate performance.                         rightTab-cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enhanced Sodium Ion Storage Behavior of P2-Type Na2/3Fe1/2Mn1/2O2 Synthesized via a Chelating Agent Assisted Route</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bai, Ying; Zhao, Lixiang; Wu, Chuan; Li, Hui; Li, Yu; Wu, Feng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">From resource abundance and low cost, high capacity layered P2-type Na2/3Fe1/2Mn1/2O2 material is regarded as a potential cathode material for Na-ion batteries but suffers from its unstable structure during cycling.  P2-type Na2/3Fe1/2Mn1/2O2 layered materials were synthesized by a chelating agent assisted sol-gel method with NH3·H2O.  With the addn. of NH3·H2O and the control of the synthesis conditions, highly active material with a more stable structure and better electrochem. performance was obtained.  Also, the influences of structure changes during different voltage ranges (1.5-4.0 V and 1.5-4.3 V vs. Na+/Na) on the Na+ storage behavior was also evaluated and compared.  When being charged to 4.2 V, an OP4-type phase emerges, which can reduce the damage by the gilding of the MeO2 layers but leads to an unstable crystal structure.  For long-term cycling, it is preferred to cut off at 4.0 V rather than at 4.3 V.  For the optimized P2-type Na2/3Fe1/2Mn1/2O2 calcined at 900°, a discharge capacity of 92 mA h/g remains after 40 cycles in the voltage range of 1.5-4.0 V, and the Coulombic efficiency remains 100%.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqEFOJ0dW4c4LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgsFMWiWdDSwA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xis1Ghug%253D%253D&amp;md5=58a1d0dd4859953759b23d8a3e32d4b8</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref11" class="rightTabRefNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit11" data-doi="10.1002/advs.201600275"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ni, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bai, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, C.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Polyanion-Type Electrode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv. Sci.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">4</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1600275</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">1600298</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2198-3844</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries, representative members of the post-lithium-battery club, are very attractive and promising for large-scale energy storage applications.  The increasing technological improvements in sodium-ion batteries (Na-ion batteries) are being driven by the demand for Na-based electrode materials that are resource-abundant, cost-effective, and long lasting.  Polyanion-type compounds are among the most promising electrode materials for Na-ion batteries due to their stability, safety, and suitable operating voltages.  The most representative polyanion-type electrode materials are Na3V2(PO4)3 and NaTi2(PO4)3 for Na-based cathode and anode materials, respectively.  Both show superior electrochemical properties and attractive prospects in terms of their development and application in Na-ion batteries.  Carbonophosphate Na3MnCO3PO4 and amorphous FePO4 have also recently emerged and are contributing to further developing the research scope of polyanion-type Na-ion batteries.  However, the typical low conductivity and relatively low capacity performance of such materials still restrict their development.  This paper presents a brief review of the research progress of polyanion-type electrode materials for Na-ion batteries, summarizing recent accomplishments, highlighting emerging strategies, and discussing the remaining challenges of such systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcRzOrk04pyNfsjl5L8hwNX0fW6udTcc2ebl4GEzCZBeR7ntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC1cvgtVOitA%253D%253D&amp;md5=f4bc597b44df5e4e4c0b0a698c9a4d56</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref12" class="rightTabRefNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit12" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2016.06.096"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bai, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Structural and morphol. control is an effective approach for improvement of electrochem. performance in rechargeable batteries.  In this paper, three different morphol. Na3V2(PO4)3 (irregular shaped, the porous sponge-like and plate like) were successfully prepd. through controlling the amt. of oxalic acid by a simple two-step redn. method.  It is found that the amt. of oxalic acid has vital impacts on the morphol. of Na3V2(PO4)3; moreover, the morphol. evolution and formation mechanism are proposed based on the reactions of different amt. of oxalic acid occurring in the two-step redn. process.  The excellent electrochem. performances of the porous sponge-like Na3V2(PO4)3 are attributed to the unique morphol.  The initial capacity of the porous sponge-like Na3V2(PO4)3 is 101.77 mAh g-1 at 30 C; after 700 cycles, it remains as high as 89.28 mAh g-1 with only 12% capacity loss.                         rightTab-cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">P2-Na0.6[Cr0.6Ti0.4]O2 cation-disordered electrode for high-rate symmetric rechargeable sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Yuesheng; Xiao, Ruijuan; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Avdeev, Maxim; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Most P2-type layered oxides exhibit Na+/vacancy-ordered superstructures because of strong Na+-Na+ interaction in the alkali metal layer and charge ordering in the transition metal layer.  These superstructures evidenced by voltage plateaus in the electrochem. curves limit the Na+ ion transport kinetics and cycle performance in rechargeable batteries.  Here we show that such Na+/vacancy ordering can be avoided by choosing the transition metal ions with similar ionic radii and different redox potentials, for example, Cr3+ and Ti4+.  The designed P2-Na0.6[Cr0.6Ti0.4]O2 is completely Na+/vacancy-disordered at any sodium content and displays excellent rate capability and long cycle life.  A sym. sodium-ion battery using the same P2-Na0.6[Cr0.6Ti0.4]O2 electrode delivers 75% of the initial capacity at 12C rate.  Our contribution demonstrates that the approach of preventing Na+/vacancy ordering by breaking charge ordering in the transition metal layer opens a simple way to design disordered electrode materials with high power d. and long cycle life.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrlj5SkXA-GV7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhy8-ctonMQDw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtF2ksrfJ&amp;md5=719e1ce8412c6a1d17ca3f1b29c3314e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref17" class="rightTabRefNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit17" data-doi="10.1021/acs.accounts.5b00482"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Luo, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shen, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bommier, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Acc.                         rightTab-cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Shen, Fei; Bommier, Clement; Zhu, Hongli; Ji, Xiulei; Hu, Liangbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">231-240</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACHRE4</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0001-4842</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The intermittent nature of renewable energy sources, such as solar and wind, calls for sustainable elec. energy storage (EES) technologies for stationary applications.  Li will be simply too rare for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) to be used for large-scale storage purposes.  In contrast, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are highly promising to meet the demand of grid-level storage because Na is truly earth abundant and ubiquitous around the globe.  Furthermore, NIBs share a similar rocking-chair operation mechanism with LIBs, which potentially provides high reversibility and long cycling life.  It would be most efficient to transfer knowledge learned on LIBs during the last three decades to the development of NIBs.  Following this logic, rapid progress has been made in NIB cathode materials, where layered metal oxides and polyanionic compds. exhibit encouraging results.  On the anode side, pure graphite as the std. anode for LIBs can only form NaC64 in NIBs if solvent co-intercalation does not occur due to the unfavorable thermodn.  In fact, it was the utilization of a carbon anode in LIBs that enabled the com. successes.  Anodes of metal-ion batteries det. key characteristics, such as safety and cycling life; thus, it is indispensable to identify suitable anode materials for NIBs.In this Account, we review recent development on anode materials for NIBs.  Due to the limited space, we will mainly discuss carbon-based and alloy-based anodes and highlight progress made in our groups in this field.  We first present what is known about the failure mechanism of graphite anode in NIBs.  We then go on to discuss studies on hard carbon anodes, alloy-type anodes, and org. anodes.  Esp., the multiple functions of natural cellulose that is used as a low-cost carbon precursor for mass prodn. and as a soft substrate for tin anodes are highlighted.  The strategies of minimizing the surface area of carbon anodes for improving the first-cycle Coulombic efficiency are also outlined, where graphene oxide was employed as dehydration agent and 2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidine-1-oxyl (TEMPO) was used to unzip wood fiber.  Furthermore, surface modification by at. layer deposition technol. is introduced, where we discover that a thin layer of Al2O3 can function to encapsulate Sn nanoparticles, leading to a much enhanced cycling performance.  We also highlight recent work about the phosphorene/graphene anode, which outperformed other anodes in terms of capacity.  The arom. org. anode is also studied as anode with very high initial sodiation capacity.  Furthermore, electrochem. intercalation of Na ions into reduced graphene oxide is applied for fabricating transparent conductors, demonstrating the great feasibility of Na ion intercalation for optical applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGorKnQvVSK3P7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhy8-ctonMQDw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtVGksrg%253D&amp;md5=90d2907a2b33fdcf59913255073dd969</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref18" class="rightTabRefNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit18" data-doi="10.1002/ijch.201400118"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bommier, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent Development on Anodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Isr.                 10.1002/ijch.201400118</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Development on Anodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bommier, Clement; Ji, Xiulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Israel Journal of Chemistry</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  New discoveries in anode materials for sodium ion batteries (NIBs) are highly necessary to achieve the goals of widespread applications, such as elec. vehicles (EVs) and grid-level energy storage.  Carbon-based materials are crit. for this task as they are inexpensive, abundant, and versatile.  They contain a plethora of structures and morphologies, ranging from highly ordered graphite or nanotubes to highly disordered amorphous carbon, thus making them very attractive for electrochem. energy storage.  This review attempts to cover past and recent progress in the development of carbon-based anode materials for NIBs.  To give a larger context, the article will briefly cover other anode materials for NIBs as well.  The aim of this paper is to provide a timely update for researchers currently involved in the resp. fields or to serve as a starting point for individuals who would like to gain a greater knowledge of new NIB anode materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpQXhpe-bbd8rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhy8-ctonMQDw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsVaqtbk%253D&amp;md5=c937a91d24c3ff50c8a97cb7302a09cc</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref19" class="rightTabRefNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit19" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b00861"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bai, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.                         rightTab-cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hard Carbon Originated from Polyvinyl Chloride Nanofibers As High-Performance Anode Material for Na-Ion Battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bai, Ying; Wang, Zhen; Wu, Chuan; Xu, Rui; Wu, Feng; Liu, Yuanchang; Li, Hui; Li, Yu; Lu, Jun; Amine, Khalil</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Two types of hard carbon materials were synthesized by direct pyrolysis of com. polyvinyl chloride (PVC) particles and pyrolysis of PVC nanofibers at 600-800°, resp., where the nanofibers were prepd. by an electrospinning PVC precursors method.  These as-prepd. hard carbon samples were used as anode materials for Na-ion batteries.  The hard carbon obtained from PVC nanofibers had a high reversible capacity of 271 mA-h/g and an initial coulombic efficiency of 69.9%, which were much superior to the one from com. PVC, namely, a reversible capacity of 206 mA-h/g and an initial coulombic efficiency of 60.9%.  In addn., the hard carbon obtained from the PVC nanofibers exhibited good cycling stability and rate performance: the initial discharge capacities were 389, 228, 194, 178, 147 mA-h/g at the c.d. of 12, 24, 60, 120, and 240 mA/g, resp., retaining 211 mA-h/g after 150 cycles.  Such excellent cycle performance, high reversible capacity, and good rate capability enabled this hard carbon to be a promising candidate as anode material for Na-ion battery application.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrPaHT0FB4U5bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhy8-ctonMQDw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXivFWhsb4%253D&amp;md5=cf8d94dcf1d6a21870eb496890ead8af</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref20" class="rightTabRefNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit20" data-doi="10.1039/C6RA27212F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bai, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Pursuing low-cost and high-performance anode materials is of great importance for developing practical sodium ion batteries.  In this work, mille-feuille shaped hard carbons derived from low-cost and environmentally friendly polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP) nanofibres are fabricated via simple electrostatic spinning and followed by further pyrolysis at 800-1200 °C, as anode materials for sodium ion batteries.  The optimized sample HC-1000 carbonized at 1000 °C shows better particle size and low surface area, and achieves a good reversible capacity of 271 mA h g-1 with 94% capacity retention ratio over 100 cycles.  In addn., HC-1000 exhibits satisfactory rate performance, namely, the discharge capacities are 304, 264, 209, 142, 109 and 70 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 20, 40, 100, 200, 500 and 1000 mA g-1 after 10 cycles resp.  Even continuing with an addnl. 280 cycles at 200 mA g-1, the capacity retains 285 mA h g-1 when the current recovers to 20 mA g-1.  The mille-feuille shaped morphol., uniform particle size distribution and low surface area enable excellent electrochem. performances of PVP based hard carbon, which is expected to be a promising anode material for Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqsc4fA9VaAMrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhy8-ctonMQDw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtVCqsrw%253D&amp;md5=5d1f7ede56c8a28483fa69aed85eb0ed</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref21" class="rightTabRefNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit21" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201502197"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fang, Y.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries are emerging as one of the most promising energy storage technologies, particularly for grid-level applications.  Among anode candidate materials, hard carbon is very attractive due to its high capacity and low cost.  However, hard carbon anodes often suffer a low first-cycle coulombic efficiency and fast capacity fading.  In this study, we discover that doping graphene oxide into sucrose, the precursor for hard carbon, can effectively reduce the sp. surface area of hard carbon to as low as 5.4 m2/g.  We further show that such doping can effectively prevent foaming during caramelization of sucrose and extend the pyrolysis burnoff of sucrose caramel over a wider temp. range.  The obtained low-surface-area hard carbon greatly improves the first-cycle coulombic efficiency from 74% to 83% and delivers a very stable cycle life with 95% of capacity retention after 200 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrqaHV_SLIfobVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhJ0RctCBz8Mw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXks1KlsQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=4b387ca5b76e59789c78130fa5560be4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref24" class="rightTabRefNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit24" data-doi="10.1039/C5TA05118E"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, B.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Facile Synthesis of High Performance Hard Carbon Anode Materials for Sodium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A highly reversible, resource-abundant and low-cost anode is indispensable to the future success of sodium ion batteries (SIBs) in large-scale energy storage application.  The authors report the facile synthesis of a biomass-derived hard carbon for SIBs by 1-step pyrolysis of shaddock peel under an inert atm. without activation or any further treatments.  The pyrolytic carbon shows very high reversible sodium storage capacities up to 430.5 mA h/g at a c.d. of 30 mA/g and superior cycling stability with only 2.5% capacity loss over 200 charge-discharge cycles.  The high capacity and excellent cycle performance, combined with the facile synthesis procedure make it a promising anode material for practical SIBs.  The good Na-ion storage property of the shaddock peel-derived pyrolytic carbon is attributed to its unique honeycomb-like morphol. with a shortened distance for Na-ion diffusion, and the large interlayer distance which is available for sodium insertion/extn.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrUZb0nR_lduLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhJ0RctCBz8Mw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsVegtr7O&amp;md5=32487f69bba862d984793316e10ff8e4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref25" class="rightTabRefNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit25" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201701835"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, Q.         </div><div class="casAbstract">This review describes the overall research activities focused on developing high-performance Li-ion batteries (LIBs) fabricated with various TiO2 polymorphs as insertion anodes.  Although several polymorphs of TiO2 have been reported, only the anatase, rutile, bronze, and brookite phases have proven promising.  The bronze phase's lower insertion potential, high reversibility and high current performance makes it an attractive candidate for constructing high power and high energy d. Li-ion power packs.  In addn., the bronze phase exhibits superior performance over the conventional, commercialized spinel Li4Ti5O12 anodes when coupled with the olivine phase LiFePO4.  This exceptional behavior of the bronze phase opens new avenues for the development of high power LIBs capable of powering zero emission transportation and grid storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpLQhs3JFpjc7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhJ0RctCBz8Mw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXjvVyhtbk%253D&amp;md5=eca06de04b707546fd8d67b86a9e25a5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref27" class="rightTabRefNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit27" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201600845"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, K. The exposed large interlayers provide facile channels for Na+ insertion and extn. when this material is used as anode for Na-ion batteries (NIBs).
