        </div><div class="casAbstract">A core-shell-shell heterostructure of Si nanoparticles as the core with mesoporous carbon and cryst. TiO2 as the double shells (Si@C@TiO2) is utilized as an anode material for lithium-ion batteries, which could successfully tackle the vital setbacks of Si anode materials, in terms of intrinsic low cond., unstable solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) films, and serious vol. variations.  Combined with the high theor. capacity of the Si core (4200 mA h g-1), the double shells can perfectly avoid direct contact of Si with electrolyte, leading to stable SEI films and enhanced Coulombic efficiency.  On the other hand, the carbon inner shell is effective at improving the overall cond. of the Si-based electrode; the TiO2 outer shell is expected to serve as a rigid layer to achieve high structural stability and integrity of the core-shell-shell structure.  As a result, the elaborate Si@C@TiO2 core-shell-shell nanoparticles are proven to show excellent Li storage properties.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Amorphous TiO2@C nanospheres were synthesized via a template approach.  After being sintered under different conditions, two types of polyphase TiO2 hollow nanospheres were obtained.  The electrochem. properties of the amorphous TiO2 nanospheres and the TiO2 hollow nanospheres with different phases were characterized as anodes for the Na-ion batteries.  It was found that all the samples demonstrated excellent cyclability, which was sustainable for hundreds of cycles with little capacity fading, although the anatase TiO2 presented a capability that was better than that of the mixed anatase/rutile TiO2 or the amorphous TiO2@C.  Through crystallog. anal., it was revealed that the anatase TiO2 crystal structure supplies two-dimensional diffusion paths for Na-ion intercalation and more accommodation sites.  D. functional theory calcns. indicated lower energy barriers for the insertion of Na+ into anatase TiO2.  Therefore, anatase TiO2 hollow nanospheres show excellent high-rate performance.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Nanosized TiO2 is now actively developed as a low-cost and potentially high capacity anode material of Na-ion batteries, but its poor capacity utilization and insufficient cyclability remains an obstacle for battery applications.  To overcome these drawbacks, electrospun TiO2/C nanofibers are synthesized, where anatase TiO2 nanocrystals with a diam. of ∼12 nm were densely embedded in the conductive carbon fibers, thus preventing them from aggregating and attacking by electrolyte.  Due to its abundant active surfaces of well-dispersed TiO2 nanocrytals and high electronic cond. of the carbon matrix, the TiO2/C anode shows a high redox capacity of ∼302.4 mA h g-1 and a high-rate capability of 164.9 mAh g-1 at a very high current of 2000 mA g-1.  More significantly, this TiO2/C anode can be cycled with nearly 100% capacity retention over 1000 cycles, showing a sufficiently long cycle life for battery applications.  The nanofibrous architecture of the TiO2/C composite and its superior electrochem. performance may provide new insights for development of better host materials for practical Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoCpHAcLSLQBbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgVj2esbt-XZA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XpvFyksbs%253D&amp;md5=8d1d90e0be7950d635c188eadb00aaed</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref35" class="rightTabRefNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit35" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b03270"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, B.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Pristine and boron-doped anatase TiO2 were prepd. via a facile sol-gel method and the hydrothermal method for application as anode materials in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  The sol-gel method leads to agglomerated TiO2, whereas the hydrothermal method is conducive to the formation of highly cryst. and discrete nanoparticles.  The structure, morphol., and electrochem. properties were studied.  The crystal size of TiO2 with boron doping is smaller than that of the nondoped crystals, which indicates that the addn. of boron can inhibit the crystal growth.  The electrochem. measurements demonstrated that the reversible capacity of the B-doped TiO2 is higher than that for the pristine sample.  B-doping also effectively enhances the rate performance.  The capacity of the B-doped TiO2 could reach 150 mAh/g at the high current rate of 2C and the capacity decay is only about 8 mAh/g over 400 cycles.  The remarkable performance could be attributed to the lattice expansion resulting from B doping and the shortened Li+ diffusion distance due to the nanosize.  These results indicate that B-doped TiO2 can be a good candidate for SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqa4lL9nW9zibVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgVj2esbt-XZA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xpt1aruro%253D&amp;md5=d6d050542c7f7e1438212730be56e3df</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref36" class="rightTabRefNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit36" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b02724"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jiao, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yuan, H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ultrasmall TiO<sub>2</sub> Nanoparticles in Situ Growth on Graphene Hybrid as Superior Anode Material for Sodium/Lithium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">To inhibit the aggregation of TiO2 nanoparticles and to improve the electrochem. kinetics of TiO2 electrode, a hybrid material of ultra-small TiO2 nanoparticles in situ grown on rGO nanosheets was obtained by ultrasonic and reflux methods.                         rightTab-cas_cit38R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">38</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Shape-Controlled TiO2 Nanocrystals for Na-Ion Battery Electrodes: The Role of Different Exposed Crystal Facets on the Electrochemical Properties</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Longoni, Gianluca; Pena Cabrera, Rosita Lissette; Polizzi, Stefano; D'Arienzo, Massimiliano; Mari, Claudio Maria; Cui, Yi; Ruffo, Riccardo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">17</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable sodium-ion batteries are becoming a viable alternative to lithium-based technol. in energy storage strategies, due to the wide abundance of sodium raw material.  In the past decade, this has generated a boom of research interest in such systems.  Notwithstanding the large no. of research papers concerning sodium-ion battery electrodes, the development of a low-cost, well-performing anode material remains the largest obstacle to overcome.  Although the well-known anatase, one of the allotropic forms of natural TiO2, was recently proposed for such applications, the material generally suffers from reduced cyclability and limited power, due to kinetic drawbacks and to its poor charge transport properties.  A systematic approach in the morphol. tuning of the anatase nanocrystals is needed, to optimize its structural features toward the electrochem. properties and to promote the material interaction with the conductive network and the electrolyte.  Aiming to face with these issues, a fine tuning was obtained of the nanoparticle morphol. and to expose the most favorable nanocrystal facets to the electrolyte and to the conductive wrapping agent (graphene), thus overcoming the intrinsic limits of anatase transport properties.  The result is a TiO2-based composite electrode able to deliver an outstandingly stability over cycles (150 mA h g-1 for more than 600 cycles in the 1.5-0.1 V potential range) never achieved with such a low content of carbonaceous substrate (5%).  Moreover, it has been demonstrated for the first time that these outstanding performances are not simply related to the overall surface area of the different morphologies but have to be directly related to the peculiar surface characteristics of the crystals.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoWUF1Ch-SrS7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgVj2esbt-XZA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XitFGgt7bF&amp;md5=92ab923e7508095d476b0f67574700d4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref39"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref39" class="rightTabRefNumLink">39</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit39" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201700129"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jiang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shi, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yu, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Multichannel Porous TiO<sub>2</sub> Hollow Nanofibers with Rich Oxygen Vacancies and High Grain Boundary Density Enabling Superior Sodium Storage Performance</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Small</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">13</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1700129</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">1700136</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">TiO2(B) anode for Li-ion batteries is prepd. by the hydrothermal method.  Styrene butadiene rubber and Na carboxyl Me cellulose (SBR/CMC) and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVdF) binders are used to prep. the TiO2(B) electrodes.  Scanning electron microscope and electrochem. impedance spectroscopy show that the electrode prepd. with SBR/CMC has better electrode maintainability and electrochem. kinetics which result in better electrochem. performance.  The optimized SBR/CMC binder content probably is at 12-15%.  The 1 M LiPF6 electrolyte dissolved in EC:DMC = 3:7 is more suitable for the TiO2(B) electrode.  Using this suggested binder content and electrolyte, the TiO2(B) material exhibits superior capacity retention and rate capability.  Even at the 10 C rate, the material still shows a discharge capacity of 142.5 mA-h g-1 which keeps well after 800 cycles.  Based on this work, SBR/CMC is a promising binder for the TiO2(B) anode which provides better electrochem. performance and is cheaper and more environmentally friendly than PVdF.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr_5SWs0CxbO7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg44osWEp0KSQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhsFCqtLjM&amp;md5=1987743dce20133f883ee4ce24c7c01d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref41" class="rightTabRefNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit41" data-doi="10.1039/c2ee22292b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Han, Z.-J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shimomura, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Murase, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yui, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Capacity Si–graphite Composite Electrodes with a Self-Formed Porous Structure by a Partially Neutralized Polyacrylate for Li-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Energy Environ.         </div><div class="casAbstract">NaOH-neutralized poly(acrylic acid) is examd. as a binder for Si-based neg. electrodes in rechargeable Li-ion batteries.  To better understand the influence of polymer binder characteristics, the impact of neutralization degree (ND) of poly(acrylic acid) (PAH) on electrode performance of a Si-graphite composite is studied.  The electrode performance of the Si-based composites is remarkably improved by appropriate neutralization of PAH because the NDs highly influence rheol. properties of a slurry and thus the electrode morphol., such as porosity.  It is found that 80% neutralized PAH provides the moderate porous structure inside the composite electrode with 10 wt% binder content because of a unique rheol. property during the drying process after casting the slurry onto a current collector.  The self-formed porous structure by the partially neutralized PAH is beneficial to buffer vol. expansion caused by lithiation of Si.  Therefore, an excellent capacity retention for a 100 cycle test with a high reversible capacity of approx. 1000 mA h g-1 is achieved with the 80% neutralized PAH as a binder for Si-based composite electrodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpi61_sqKRbprVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg44osWEp0KSQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38Xhtlyqs7vJ&amp;md5=cb5b9f17dc1fe9a8951b4b4ec2e05b8d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref42" class="rightTabRefNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit42" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201201568"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Koo, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cho, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The identification of similarities in the material requirements for applications of interest and those of living organisms provides opportunities to use renewable natural resources to develop better materials and design better devices.  In the authors' work, the authors harness this strategy to build high-capacity Si nanopowder-based Li-ion batteries with improved performance characteristics.  Si offers more than one order of magnitude higher capacity than graphite, but it exhibits dramatic vol. changes during electrochem. alloying and de-alloying with Li, which typically leads to rapid anode degrdn.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The reduced graphene/poly(vinylidene fluoride) nanocomposite films were prepd. by the soln. casting-thermal redn. process using graphene oxide and poly(vinylidene fluoride) resin.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We report the electrochem. activity of anatase TiO2 nanorods in a Na cell.  The anatase TiO2 nanorods were synthesized by a hydrothermal method, and their surfaces were coated by carbon to improve the elec. cond. by carbonization of pitch at 700° for 2 h in Ar flow.  The resulting structure does not change before and after the carbon coating, as confirmed by x-ray diffraction.  Transmission electron microscopy images confirm the presence of a carbon coating on the anatase TiO2 nanorods.  In cell tests, anodes of bare and carbon-coated anatase TiO2 nanorods exhibit stable cycling performance and attain a capacity of about 172 and 193 mA-h/g on the first charge, resp., in the voltage range of 0-3 V.  With the help of the conductive carbon layers, the carbon-coated anatase TiO2 delivers more capacity at high rates, 104 mA-h/g at the 10C rate (3.3 A/g), 82 mA-h/g at the 30C rate (10 A/g), and 53 mA-h/g at the 100C rate (33 A g-1).  By contrast, the anode of bare anatase TiO2 nanorods delivers only about 38 mA-h/g at the 10C rate (3.3 A/g).  The excellent cyclability and high-rate capability are the result of a Na+ insertion and extn. reaction into the host structure coupled with Ti4+/3+ redox reaction, as shown by x-ray absorption spectroscopy.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpadjcIpWM-W7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhWn6cyBh7ZjA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXktlGhsA%253D%253D&amp;md5=4bc3a520e6f654a82cbee3b778eef0e7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref49" class="rightTabRefNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit49" data-doi="10.1021/am501772a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Oh, S.-M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hwang, J.-Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yoon, C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">With the aim of advancing anatase TiO2 anodes for Na ion batteries, cryst. TiO2 nanocubes were employed and they delivered a gradually increasing capacity during the initial cycles, termed as an activation process.  The no. of necessary discharge-charge loops for total activation is dependent on the galvanostatic c.d. (∼20 cycles at 0.2 C, or 90 cycles at 1 C).  A percentage of Ti3+ was detected after the activation, indicating an amt. of irreversibly trapped Na ions in the lattice.  After the activation process, an excellent rate capability and outstanding cycling stability were presented.  The reversible capacity reached 174, 132, and 108 mA h g-1 at rates of 1 C, 5 C, and 10 C, resp.  The capacity was sustained with a loss of &lt;10% after 1000 discharge-charge cycles at a rate of 2 C or 10 C.  The superior battery performance achieved by the nanocubes is related to the encircled {100} facets that are more favorable for Na ion attachment compared to the {001} and {101} facets, as supported by 1st-principles calcns.  From this work the authors can see the feasibility of optimizing electrode materials via rational surface structure construction based on theor. calcns.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpebO59ke4jqbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhWn6cyBh7ZjA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXkvFGjsrw%253D&amp;md5=a5ab3c8c5cb9855d41e67b06bcde30de</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref52"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref52" class="rightTabRefNumLink">52</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit52" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms7929"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are emerging as a highly promising technol. for large-scale energy storage applications.  However, it remains a significant challenge to develop an anode with superior long-term cycling stability and high-rate capability.  Here we demonstrate that the Na+ intercalation pseudocapacitance in TiO2/graphene nanocomposites enables high-rate capability and long cycle life in a sodium-ion battery.  This hybrid electrode exhibits a specific capacity of above 90 mA h g-1 at 12,000 mA g-1 (∼36 C).  The capacity is highly reversible for more than 4,000 cycles, the longest demonstrated cyclability to date.  First-principle calcns. demonstrate that the intimate integration of graphene with TiO2 reduces the diffusion energy barrier, thus enhancing the Na+ intercalation pseudocapacitive process.  The Na-ion intercalation pseudocapacitance enabled by tailor-deigned nanostructures represents a promising strategy for developing electrode materials with high power d. and long cycle life.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrGrV7K8Tq5mLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhWn6cyBh7ZjA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtF2lurzK&amp;md5=99303fd99ef59044874690750282067a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref53" class="rightTabRefNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit53" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.5b00633"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wen, L.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (SIBs) represent an effective energy storage technol. with potentially lower material costs than Li ion batteries.  Here, the electrochem. performance of SIBs, esp. rate capability, is intimately connected to the electrode design at the nanoscale by taking anatase TiO2 as an example.  Highly ordered 3-dimensional (3D) Ni-TiO2 core-shell nanoarrays were fabricated using nanoimprited AAO templating technique and directly used as anode.  The nanoarrays delivered a reversible capacity of ∼200 mA h g-1 after 100 cycles at the c.d. of 50 mA h g-1 and were able to retain a capacity of ∼95 mA h g-1 at the c.d. ≤5 A g-1 and fully recover low rate capacity.  High ion accessibility, fast electron transport, and excellent electrode integrity were shown as great merits to obtain the presented electrochem. performance.  The work demonstrates the possibility of highly ordered 3-dimensional heterostructured nanoarrays as a promising electrode design for Na energy storage to alleviate the reliance on the materials' intrinsic nature and provides a versatile and cost-effective technique for the fabrication of such perfectly ordered nanostructures.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoHslR0Alvw9bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhWn6cyBh7ZjA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXpsFOjuro%253D&amp;md5=2843c505872489891b1e7f89f12fa7a3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref54" class="rightTabRefNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit54" data-doi="10.1038/srep11960"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hong, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wei, M.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Iso-Oriented Anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> Mesocages as a High Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Storage</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Sci.                         rightTab-cas_cit54R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">54</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Iso-Oriented Anatase TiO2 Mesocages as a High Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hong Zhensheng; Zhou Kaiqiang; Huang Zhigao; Wei Mingdeng; Wei Mingdeng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Scientific reports</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11960</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">A major obstacle in realizing Na-ion batteries (NIBs) is the absence of suitable anode materials.  Herein, we firstly report the anatase TiO2 mesocages constructed by crystallographically oriented nanoparticle subunits as a high performance anode for NIBs.  The mesocages with tunable microstructures, high surface area (204 m(2) g(-1)) and uniform mesoporous structure were firstly prepared by a general synthesis method under the assist of sodium dodecyl sulfate (SDS).  It's notable that the TiO2 mesocages exhibit a large reversible capacity and good rate capability.  A stable capacity of 93 mAhg(-1) can be retained after 500 cycles at 10 C in the range of 0.01-2.5 V, indicating high rate performance and good cycling stability.  This could be due to the uniform architecture of iso-oriented mesocage structure with few grain boundaries and nanoporous nature, allowing fast electron and ion transport, and providing more active sites as well as freedom for volume change during Na-ion insertion.  CV measurements demonstrate that the sodium-ion storage process of anatase mesocages is mainly controlled by pseudocapacitive behavior, which is different from the lithium-ion storage and further facilitates the high rate capability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcQNy5_7QVOqG5Pq5LRczm8ffW6udTcc2ebGhBwRPRSccrntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC28%252FgvFWrtQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=0121d7bef5cb80893b9c3204ed716cc4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref55"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref55" class="rightTabRefNumLink">55</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit55" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201501489"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tahir, M.                 10.1002/aenm.201501489</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Extraordinary Performance of Carbon-Coated Anatase TiO2 as Sodium-Ion Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tahir Muhammad Nawaz; Panthofer Martin; Tremel Wolfgang; Oschmann Bernd; Buchholz Daniel; Dou Xinwei; Passerini Stefano; Lieberwirth Ingo; Zentel Rudolf</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced energy materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1501489</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6832</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">The synthesis of in situ polymer-functionalized anatase TiO2 particles using an anchoring block copolymer with hydroxamate as coordinating species is reported, which yields nanoparticles (≈11 nm) in multigram scale.  Thermal annealing converts the polymer brushes into a uniform and homogeneous carbon coating as proven by high resolution transmission electron microscopy and Raman spectroscopy.  The strong impact of particle size as well as carbon coating on the electrochemical performance of anatase TiO2 is demonstrated.  Downsizing the particles leads to higher reversible uptake/release of sodium cations per formula unit TiO2 (e.g., 0.72 eq.  Na(+) (11 nm) vs only 0.56 eq.  Na(+) (40 nm)) while the carbon coating improves rate performance.  The combination of small particle size and homogeneous carbon coating allows for the excellent electrochemical performance of anatase TiO2 at high (134 mAh g(-1) at 10 C (3.35 A g(-1))) and low (≈227 mAh g(-1) at 0.1 C) current rates, high cycling stability (full capacity retention between 2nd and 300th cycle at 1 C) and improved coulombic efficiency (≈99.8%).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcRca10rw1_Tc0Gy1nwu7K2mfW6udTcc2eavxkU5CH8BW7ntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC1c%252Fht1Wltw%253D%253D&amp;md5=29c3c300fc394f1c426270e5e28f600e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref56" class="rightTabRefNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit56" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201504412"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ni, J.                         rightTab-cas_cit56R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">56</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Self-Supported Nanotube Arrays of Sulfur-Doped TiO2 Enabling Ultrastable and Robust Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ni, Jiangfeng; Fu, Shidong; Wu, Chao; Maier, Joachim; Yu, Yan; Li, Liang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADVMEW</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We, for the 1st time, combine the unique nanotubular architecture with high cond. by engineering self-supported nanotube arrays of S-doped TiO2 (S-TiO2).  Such doping by isovalent substitution can be helpful owing to either elastic effect or, at high enough concn., to variation of materials const. (e.g., bandgap).  S-doped TiO2 materials have been reported and characterized, yet never used as electrodes for Na-based battery behaviors.  The increase of electronic cond. could be predominately ascribed to structure affects rather than proper doping, as shown by the strong effect on the band gap.  An addnl. possibility is the introduction of an anionic deficiency (anion vacancies compensated by electron due to prepn.).  Note the deoxygenation effect of S treatment, an aliovalent effect as, e.g., achieved by donor doping TiO2, may be excluded here.  The S-TiO2 nanotube array features intrinsic merits for Na storage.  First, the self-supported nanotube array can be directly adapted for electrode without using binder and C additive.  Second, the ordered nanotube architecture exhibits large interface and channels for transfer of Na ion during cycling.  Third, the S doping drastically boosts the electron movement within the nanotubes.  Consequently, the S-TiO2 nanotubes enable superior Na storage in terms of high capacity (320 mAh/g), ultrastable cycling (91% retention over 4400 cycles), and robust rate capability (167 mAh/g at 3.35 A/g).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrObwc1x3fRibVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljpH7EZ4RAEKA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xht1ektbc%253D&amp;md5=cf8583f1f9e0593bbc7f6af9a5227e30</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref57"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref57" class="rightTabRefNumLink">57</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit57" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201600606"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, X.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In summary, nitrogen-doped ordered mesoporous TiO2 nanofibers (N-MTO) have been fabricated by electrospinning method and subsequent nitridation treatment.  When used as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries, the N-MTO anode exhibits superior cycling stability and rate performance.  The 1D N-MTO contains nanosized TiO2 particles and pores that not only shorten the diffusion length of Na ions in TiO2 but also allow sufficient infiltration of electrolyte and allow fast diffusion of Na ions.  The introduction of N into TiO2 results in partial formation of Ti3+ assocd. with oxygen vacancies, leading to reduced sodium ion and electron transport diffusion resistance, which improves the rate capability at high rates.  The N-MTO shows an excellent rate capability (110 mAh/g at 10 C) and long cycle life (only 6% capacity loss after 500 cycles at a rate of 10 C).  The proposed N-MTO hybrid could serve as a model for simple modification of other metal oxide (i.e., CoO, Fe2O3) materials derived from electrospinning for potential application in energy storage systems and other field (such as photocatalysis).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpnXuplwQMaG7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljpH7EZ4RAEKA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XotFOjt7k%253D&amp;md5=4fbefc1171bed1cd934f6a3165e25f72</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref58"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref58" class="rightTabRefNumLink">58</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit58" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2017.05.040"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, W.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-ion batteries have attracted enormous attention for large-scale and sustainable energy storage applications.  Here the authors present a design of hierarchical Li3V2(PO4)3/C mesoporous nanowires via 1-pot synthesis process.  The mesoporous structure is directly in situ carbonized from the surfactants (CTAB and oxalic acid) along with the crystn. of Li3V2(PO4)3 without using any hard templates.  As a cathode for lithium-ion battery, the Li3V2(PO4)3/C mesoporous nanowires exhibit outstanding high-rate and ultralong-life performance with capacity retention of 80.0% after 3000 cycles at 5 C in 3-4.3 V.  Even at 10 C, it still delivers 88.0% of its theor. capacity.  The ability to provide this level of performance is attributed to the hierarchical mesoporous nanowires with bicontinuous electron/ion pathways, large electrode-electrolyte contact area, low charge transfer resistance, and robust structure stability upon prolonged cycling.  The unique mesoporous nanowires structure is favorable for improving the cyclability and rate capability in energy storage applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpe62JH_xzkX7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljpH7EZ4RAEKA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXpt1KksQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=aafed2e144d4eae1cd187ee83893a458</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref63"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref63" class="rightTabRefNumLink">63</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit63" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201302915"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Raji, A.-R.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The slow kinetics of bigger-sized sodium ions in intercalation compds. restricts the practical applications of sodium batteries.  In this work, sodium ion intercalation/deintercalation behavior of Na0.44MnO2 (NMO), which is one of the promising cathode materials for sodium batteries, is presented in both aq. and non-aq. electrolyte systems.  The NMO samples synthesized using modified Pechini method shows better rate capability in 0.5 M sodium sulfate aq. electrolyte system than the 1 M sodium perchlorate non-aq. system.  The difference in the rate performance is extensively investigated using electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements and the apparent diffusion coeffs. of sodium in NMO are detd. to be in the range of 1.08 × 10-13 to 9.15 × 10-12 cm2 s-1 in aq. system and in the range of 5.75 × 10-16 to 2.14 × 10-14 cm2 s-1 in non-aq. systems.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-capacity SiO powder composite electrodes for rechargeable Li-ion batteries are prepd. with different polymer binders of poly(acrylic acid) (PAA), poly(vinyl alc.) (PVA), Na CM-cellulose (CMCNa), and conventional poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVdF).  Electrode performance of the SiO composites depends on the binder, and their electrochem. reversibility is improved by using PAA as the binder in comparison to the PVdF, CMCNa, and PVA binders.  Coulombic efficiency at the initial cycle is improved for the SiO-PAA composite electrode, and the reversible capacity reaches 700-750 mA-h/g for continuous fifty cycling test at a rate of 100 mA/g.  The improvement mechanism of SiO-PAA composite electrode is characterized by x-ray diffraction, electron microscopy, XPS, IR spectroscopy, self-discharge test, and adhesive strength test.  Amorphous PAA polymer not only tightly binds but also covers the individual SiO particles.  Also, the PAA binder suppresses swelling of the composite electrode with the electrolyte soln. compared to the PVdF binder.  Through-thickness elec. resistance of the PAA composite electrode is much lower than that of the PVdF when it is wet with the electrolyte.  Probably these characters of the PAA binder effectively suppress isolation of the SiO powders in the composite electrode assocd. with the large vol. expansion/shrinkage during the lithiation/de-lithiation processes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrfWvZ1NtMFrrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhV7B2046hV2Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXns1Knurs%253D&amp;md5=5a172e0505ab2e5895bff686cd9f67b5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref67"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref67" class="rightTabRefNumLink">67</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit67" data-doi="10.1016/j.carres.2009.12.010"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Papageorgiou, S. From the frequencies of the characteristic peaks for asym. COO stretching vibration (ν asym(COO-) and sym. COO stretching vibration (ν sym(COO-)) a pseudo bridged' unidentate coordination with intermol. hydrogen bonds is proposed for the metal-carboxylate complexes in polyguluronic regions while for the polymannuronic regions the bidentate bridging coordination was proposed.  The PIB factor introduced previously as a relationship between metal sorption and frequencies of the asym. vibrations was found not to correlate with sorption capacity or any other phys. property of the metal-alginate complexes studied.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo2ztTc_VcUgrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhV7B2046hV2Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXhtlGitbs%253D&amp;md5=4f8b16816f6412940770931ef92ce591</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref68"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref68" class="rightTabRefNumLink">68</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit68" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2013.01.010"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gong, L. Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nguyen, M. H. T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Oh, E.-S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High Polar Polyacrylonitrile as a Potential Binder for Negative Electrodes in Lithium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Electrochem. Commun.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">29</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">45</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">47</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.elecom.2013.01.010</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit68R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">68</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High polar polyacrylonitrile as a potential binder for negative electrodes in lithium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gong, Liyuan; Nguyen, Minh Hien Thi; Oh, Eun-Suok</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochemistry Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">29</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">45-47</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ECCMF9</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1388-2481</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this study, we investigated the potential of highly polar polyacrylonitrile (PAN) as a binder for neg. electrodes in lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).  The electrochem. performance of PAN on three representative active materials, com. graphite, high-capacity silicon/graphite, and high-power Li4Ti5O12 (LTO), was characterized and compared to the performance of conventional polyvinylidene fluoride (PVdF) and sodium-CM-cellulose (CMC) binders.  The high polarity of nitrile groups in PAN leads to larger binder coverage on the surface of active materials, resulting in strong adhesion and low irreversible capacity.  Addnl., PAN-contg. electrodes showed good electrolyte wettability and low charge transfer resistance.  These contribute to excellent electrochem. performance of PAN binder.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpyYZV5cHsBIbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhV7B2046hV2Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXktFajtLk%253D&amp;md5=068884b9d1eb6246a22bfd650dddf3e0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref69"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref69" class="rightTabRefNumLink">69</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit69" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201100236"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shimomura, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shimbe, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ozeki, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Son, J.-Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Oji, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Katayama, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Miura, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Graphite-Silicon-Polyacrylate Negative Electrodes in Ionic Liquid Electrolyte for Safer Rechargeable Li-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv. Energy Mater.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2011</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">1</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">759</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">765</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.201100236</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit69R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">69</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Graphite-silicon-polyacrylate negative electrodes in ionic liquid electrolyte for safer rechargeable Li-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Shimomura, Keiji; Shimbe, Yukako; Ozeki, Tomoaki; Son, Jin-Young; Oji, Hiroshi; Katayama, Yasushi; Miura, Takashi; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">759-765</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium poly(acrylate) binder efficiently improves the electrode performance of graphite/silicon composites in TFSA-based pure ionic liq.  The reversible cycling with capacity of 360 mA-h/g is achieved using a graphite-PANa electrode and increases to 1000 mA-h/g with graphite/Si-PANa composite electrodes.  The electrode-active materials are covered with PANa binder, and thus agglomeration is suppressed.  The PANa layer prevents the decompn. of IL during electrochem. cycling, which probably behaves like a SEI.  High-energy cathode materials also reversibly works in the BMPTFSA system - reported elsewhere.  The results are important for high-energy and safe lithium-ion batteries with ionic liq.-based electrolytes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp4l9hPmgjyTLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhV7B2046hV2Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhtlShtrfK&amp;md5=2ae9693b2d056e8af240a70023be8c9a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref70"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref70" class="rightTabRefNumLink">70</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit70" data-doi="10.1038/nnano.2012.35"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chan, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Choi, J. W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ryu, I.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mcdowell, M. T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, S. W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jackson, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, L.</span><span class="NLM_etal"></span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Stable Cycling of Double-Walled Silicon Nanotube Battery Anodes through Solid-Electrolyte Interphase Control</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nat. Nanotechnol.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">7</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">310</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">315</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/nnano.2012.35</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit70R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">70</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Stable cycling of double-walled silicon nanotube battery anodes through solid-electrolyte interphase control</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Hui; Chan, Gerentt; Choi, Jang Wook; Ryu, Ill; Yao, Yan; McDowell, Matthew T.; Lee, Seok Woo; Jackson, Ariel; Yang, Yuan; Hu, Liangbing; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">310-315</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NNAABX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1748-3387</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Although the performance of lithium ion-batteries continues to improve, their energy d. and cycle life remain insufficient for applications in consumer electronics, transport, and large-scale renewable energy storage.  Silicon has a large charge storage capacity and this makes it an attractive anode material, but pulverization during cycling and an unstable solid-electrolyte interphase has limited the cycle life of silicon anodes to hundreds of cycles.  Here, it is shown that anodes consisting of an active silicon nanotube surrounded by an ion-permeable silicon oxide shell can cycle over 6,000 times in half cells while retaining &gt; 85% of their initial capacity.  The outer surface of the silicon nanotube is prevented from expansion by the oxide shell, and the expanding inner surface is not exposed to the electrolyte, resulting in a stable solid-electrolyte interphase.  Batteries contg. these double-walled silicon nanotube anodes exhibit charge capacities approx. eight times larger than conventional carbon anodes and charging rates of up to 20C (a rate of 1C corresponds to complete charge or discharge in one hour).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqaEH2gqjH0ZbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhV7B2046hV2Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XksVentLc%253D&amp;md5=13d632e4d2279068afe4a35d7fe7d4c0</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i12">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.7b17659" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.7b17659</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) mapping of pristine TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes and FTIR spectra of SA/TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode (PDF) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/suppl_file/am7b17659_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b17659/suppl_file/am7b17659_si_001.pdf">am7b17659_si_001.pdf (395.48 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

	{

	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	});

	    e.preventDefault();

<script nonce="90f6e9e27dec775c-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

)|sy(01|mb)|t2(18|50)|t6(00|10|18)|ta(gt|lk)|tcl\-|tdg\-|tel(i|m)|tim\-|t\-mo|to(pl|sh)|ts(70|m\-|m3|m5)|tx\-9|up(\.b|g1|si)|utst|v400|v750|veri|vi(rg|te)|vk(40|5[0-3]|\-v)|vm40|voda|vulc|vx(52|53|60|61|70|80|81|83|85|98)|w3c(\-| )|webc|whit|wi(g |nc|nw)|wmlb|wonu|x700|yas\-|your|zeto|zte\-/i.test(a.substr(0,4))) check = true;})(navigator.userAgent||navigator.vendor||window.opera);
              return check;
        };

    var _isMobile = window.mobilecheck();
    if (_isMobile) {
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').addClass('mobile');
    }

    var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage = "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

    function init() {
        setTimeout(function() {
            var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
            if (cookieExist) {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
                return false;
            } else {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
                gdprMessage();
            }
            //Events
            $('#closebtn').on("click", hideForm);

            //Handle submit
            $('#gdpr-message-btn').on("click", function(event) {
                handleClick();
            });

            // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
            // $(document).click(function(event) {
            // 	event.stopPropagation();
            //   console.log(event.target);
            //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
            //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
            //     $(this).off();
            //   }
            // });
            $('#gdpr-con-btn').click(function() {
                setCookie("gdpr", "true");
                hideForm();
            });
        }, time);
    }

    //Show the message
    function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $('#gdpr-message-modal').show();
    }

    function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
            //Submit the form
            //Hide form
            if ($('.gdpr_err')) {
                $('.gdpr_err').hide();
            }

            $("#gdpr-message-content")
                //If successful then...
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
            //Show error message
            gdprErrorMessage();
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
    }

    function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
            return false;
        } else {
            errorStatus = true;
            $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
    }

    function validateEmail(email) {
        var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
    }

    function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
    }

    function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + '=([^;]+)'));
        if (match) return match[1];
    }

    function hideForm() { //And set cookie
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag('event', 'GDPR Modal', { 'event_category': 'Pubs GDPR', 'event_label':'GDPR Dismissal'});
    }

    return {
        init: init
    }
})();

$(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
</script>

var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
